They aren’t random. Our stories are built in often unconscious but systematic ways. First, we take in information. We experience the world — sights, sounds, and feelings. Second, we interpret what we see, hear, and feel; we tell a story that gives it all meaning. And from that we draw conclusions about what’s happening.

At each step, there is an opportunity for our stories to diverge.

Put simply, we all have different stories about the world because we each take in different information and then interpret this information in our own unique ways.

In difficult conversations, too often we trade only conclusions back and forth, without stepping down to where most of the real action is: the information and interpretations that lead each of us to see the world as we do.

1. We Have Different Information

There are two reasons we all have different information about the world. First, as each of us proceeds through life — and through any difficult situation — the information available to us is overwhelming. We simply can’t take in all of the sights, sounds, facts, and feelings involved in even a single encounter. Inevitably, we end up noticing some things and ignoring others. And what we each choose to notice and ignore will be different. Second, we each have access to different information.

We Notice Different Things. Doug took his four-year-old nephew, Andrew, to watch a homecoming parade. Sitting on his uncle’s shoulders, Andrew shouted with delight as athletes, cheerleaders, and the school band rolled by on lavish floats. Afterward Andrew exclaimed, “That was the best truck parade I’ve ever seen!”

Each float, it seems, was pulled by a truck. Andrew, truck obsessed as he was, saw nothing else. His uncle Doug, truck indifferent, hadn’t noticed a single truck. In a sense, Andrew and his uncle watched completely different parades.

Like Doug and Andrew, what we notice has to do with who we are, what we’ve experienced, and what we care about. Some of us pay more attention to feelings and relationships. Others to status and power, or to facts and logic. Some of us are artists, others are scientists, others pragmatists. Some of us want to prove we’re right; others want to avoid conflict or smooth it over. Some of us tend to see ourselves as victims, others as heroes, observers, or survivors. The information we attend to varies accordingly.

Of course, neither Doug nor Andrew walked away from the parade thinking, “I enjoyed my particular perspective on the parade based on the information I paid attention to.” Each walked away thinking, “I enjoyed the parade.” Each assumes that what he paid attention to was what was significant about the experience. Each assumes he has “the facts.”

In a more serious setting, Randy and Daniel, coworkers on an assembly line, experience the same dynamic. They’ve had a number of tense conversations about racial issues. Randy, who is White, believes that the company they work for has a generally good record on racial and ethnic diversity in both recruitment and promotion. He notices that of the seven people on their assembly team, three are White, two are Black, one is Hispanic, and one — Daniel — is Korean American. Randy is a member of a recently formed committee to promote inclusion and a sense of belonging at the company, and he has observed how much effort has gone into recruiting and retaining People of Color. Randy has done some of that recruiting himself.

Daniel has a different view. He has been on the receiving end of unusual questions about his qualifications. Over the years he has experienced racial slurs from coworkers, and has heard similar stories from others. These experiences are prominent in his mind. He also knows of several People of Color who were overlooked for promotions, and notices that a disproportionate number of the top executives at the company are White. And Daniel has listened repeatedly to executives who talk as if the only two racial categories that mattered were White and Black.

While Randy and Daniel have some information that is shared, they each have information the other does not. Yet each assumes that his view is the view. In a sense, it’s as if Randy and Daniel work at different companies.

Often we go through an entire conversation — or indeed an entire relationship — without ever realizing that each of us is paying attention to different things, and that our views are based on different information.

We Each Know Ourselves Better Than Anyone Else Can. In addition to choosing different information, we each have access to different information. For example, others have access to information about themselves that we don’t. They know the constraints they are under; we don’t. They know their hopes, dreams, losses, and wounds; we don’t. They know what it feels like to move through their world in their body, with their desires, burdens, fears, moods, insecurities, and strengths; we don’t. We act as if we’ve got access to all the important information there is to know about them, but we don’t. It’s easy to forget that their life experiences may be quite different from our own, and that their internal landscape is as rich and complex as our own.

Let’s return to the example of Jack and Michael. When Michael describes what happened, he doesn’t mention anything about Jack’s staying up all night. He might not know that Jack stayed up all night, and even if he does, his “knowledge” would be quite limited compared to what Jack knows about it. Jack was there. Jack knows what it felt like as he struggled to stay awake. He knows how uncomfortable it was when the heat was turned off at midnight. He knows how disappointed his daughter was when he canceled their dinner together. He knows about the anxiety he felt putting aside other important work to do Michael’s project. Jack also knows how happy he felt to be doing a favor for a friend.

And there is plenty that Jack is not aware of. Jack doesn’t know that Michael’s Board Chair blew up just that morning over the choice of photograph in another disclosure document that Michael had supervised. Jack doesn’t know that the earnings figures are a particularly hot topic because of questions about some of the company’s recent business decisions. Jack doesn’t know that Michael’s graphic designer has taken an unscheduled personal leave in the midst of their busiest season, affecting multiple time-sensitive projects. Jack doesn’t know that Michael has been dissatisfied with some of Jack’s work in the past. And Jack doesn’t know how happy Michael felt to be doing a favor for a friend.

Of course, in advance, we don’t know what we don’t know. But rather than assuming we already know everything we need to, we should assume that there is important information we don’t yet have. It’s a good bet to be true.

2. We Have Different Interpretations

“We never have sex,” Alvie complains in the movie Annie Hall. “We’re constantly having sex,” says his girlfriend, Annie. “How often do you have sex?” asks their therapist. “Three times a week!” they reply in unison.

A second reason we tell different stories about the world is that, even when we have the same information, we interpret it differently — we give it different meaning. I see the cup as half empty; you see it as a metaphor for the fragility of humankind. I’m thirsty; you’re a poet. Two especially important factors in how we interpret what we see are (1) our past experiences and (2) the implicit rules we’ve learned about how things should and should not be done.

We Are Influenced by Past Experiences. The past gives meaning to the present. Often, it is only in the context of someone’s past experience that we can understand why what they are saying or doing makes any kind of sense.

To celebrate the end of a long project, Darla and her coworkers scraped together the money to treat their supervisor, Carolina, to dinner at a nice restaurant. Throughout the meal, Carolina did little but complain: “Everything is overpriced.” “How can they get away with this?” And, “You’ve got to be kidding. Twelve dollars for dessert!” Darla went home embarrassed and frustrated, thinking, “We knew she was cheap, but this is ridiculous. We paid so she wouldn’t have to worry about the money, and still she complained about the cost. She ruined the evening.”

Though the story in Darla’s head was that Carolina was simply a cheapskate or a wet blanket, Darla eventually decided to ask Carolina why she had such a strong reaction to the expense of eating out. Upon reflection, Carolina explained:

I suppose it has to do with my experience as an immigrant. We moved here when I was six and we had nothing. I can still hear my mother’s voice from those first few months, as I was getting ready to go off to school in the morning. “Lina, there’s a quarter on the counter for your lunch!” she’d call. She was so proud to be able to pay for my lunch every day. Of course, that wasn’t enough to buy lunch, and I could never bring myself to tell her.

Years later, even a moderately priced meal can feel like a wasteful extravagance to Carolina when filtered through the images and feelings of this experience.

Every strong view you have is profoundly influenced by your past experiences. Where to vacation, how closely to supervise your teenagers, how long to stay at your current job — all are influenced by what you’ve observed in your own family and learned throughout your life. Often we aren’t even aware of how these experiences affect our interpretation of the world. We simply believe that this is the way things are.

We Apply Different Implicit Rules. Our past experiences often develop into “rules” by which we live our lives. Whether we are aware of them or not, we all follow such rules. They tell us how the world works, how people should act, or how things are supposed to be. And they have a significant influence on the story we tell about what is happening between us in a difficult conversation.

We get into trouble when our rules collide.

Ollie and Thelma, for example, are stuck in a tangle of conflicting rules. As sales representatives, they spend a lot of time together on the road. One evening, they agreed to meet at 7:00 the next morning in the hotel lobby to finish preparing a presentation they’d be giving at 9:00. Thelma, as usual, arrived at 7:00 sharp. Ollie showed up at 7:10. This was not the first time Ollie had arrived late, and Thelma was so frustrated that she had trouble focusing for the first twenty minutes of their meeting. Ollie was frustrated that Thelma was frustrated.

It helps to clarify the implicit rules that each is unconsciously applying. Thelma’s rule is “It is unprofessional and inconsiderate to be late.” Ollie’s rule is “It is unprofessional to obsess about small things so much that you can’t focus on what’s important.” Because Thelma and Ollie both interpret the situation through the lens of their own implicit rule, they each see the other person as acting inappropriately.

Our implicit rules often take the form of things people “should” or “shouldn’t” do: “You should spend money on education, but not on clothes.” “You should never criticize a colleague in front of others.” “You should never leave the toilet seat up, squeeze the toothpaste in the middle, or let the kids have sugar before bedtime.” The list is endless.

There’s nothing wrong with having these rules. They often come from our experiences growing up — from what we see around us in our family and in our culture. We need such rules to order our lives. But when you find yourself in conflict, it helps to make your rules explicit and to encourage the other person to do the same. This greatly reduces the chance that you will be caught in an accidental duel of conflicting rules.

3. Our Conclusions Reflect Self-Interest

Finally, when we think about why we each tell our own stories about the world, there is no getting around the fact that our conclusions are partisan, that they often reflect our self-interest. We are more attuned to information that is consistent with our story of the world, and we search — often unconsciously — for information that supports our particular view. When we find it, we give that information the most favorable interpretation we reasonably can. Then, reflecting on what we have noticed, we feel even more certain that our view is right.

Professor Howard Raiffa of the Harvard Business School demonstrated this phenomenon when he gave teams of people a set of facts about a company. He told some of the teams they would be negotiating to buy the company, and others that they would be selling the company. He then asked each team to value the company as objectively as possible (not the price at which they would offer to buy or sell, but what they believed it was actually worth). Raiffa found that sellers, in their heart of hearts, believed the company to be worth 30 percent more on average than the independently assessed fair market value. Buyers, on average, valued it at 30 percent less than the assessment.

Each team developed a self-serving perception without realizing they were doing so. They focused more on things that were consistent with what they wanted to believe and tended to ignore, explain away, and soon forget those that weren’t. Our colleague Roger Fisher captured this phenomenon in a wry reflection on his days as a litigator: “I sometimes failed to persuade the court that I was right, but I never failed to persuade myself!”

This tendency to develop unconsciously biased perceptions is very human, and can be dangerous. It calls for a dose of humility about the “rightness” of our story, especially when we have something important at stake.

Move from Certainty to Curiosity

There’s only one way to come to understand the other person’s story, and that’s by being curious. Instead of asking yourself, “How can they be so selfish?!” ask yourself, “I wonder what information they have that I don’t?” Instead of asking, “How can they be so irrational?” ask, “How might they see the world such that their view makes sense? What important experiences have shaped them?” Certainty locks us out of their story; curiosity lets us in.

Curiosity: The Way into Their Story

Consider the disagreement between Tony and his wife, Keiko. Tony’s sister has just given birth to her first child. The next day Keiko is getting ready to visit the hospital. To her shock, Tony says he’s not going with her, but instead is planning to work in their garden. When Keiko asks why, Tony mumbles something about this being “time sensitive” and “the only time he has to plant,” and says that he’ll stop by the hospital tomorrow.

Keiko is deeply troubled by this, thinking, “What kind of person thinks gardening is more important than family? That’s the most selfish, ridiculous thing I’ve ever heard!” But she catches herself in her own certainty, and instead of saying, “How could you make such a choice?” she negotiates herself to a place of curiosity. She wonders what Tony knows that she doesn’t: “How is he seeing the world such that his decision makes sense to him?”

The story Tony tells is different from what Keiko had imagined. From the outside, Tony is just puttering in the garden, as he does periodically. But to Tony it’s a matter of his mental health. Throughout the week, he works ten hours a day under extremely stressful conditions, then comes home and plays with his two boys, doing whatever they want. After the struggle of getting them to bed, he spends time with Keiko, talking mostly about what happened in Keiko’s world that day. Finally, he collapses into bed.

For Tony, Sunday is the one day during the week when he can truly relax for a bit, and working in the garden is particularly nourishing for him. His stress level goes down, almost as if he’s meditating, and this three hours to himself has a significant impact on his ability to take on the week ahead and be there for his family. Also, Tony knows that certain things need to get done now for the garden to flourish through the summer, and not getting to them this weekend would add to his stress level. Since Tony also believes that his sister won’t care whether he comes today or tomorrow (and might even prefer to spread visits out), he chooses in favor of his mental health (though not without a bit of guilt).

Of course, that’s not the end of the issue. Keiko needs to share her story with Tony, and then, once everything is on the table, together they can figure out what to do. But that will never happen if Keiko simply assumes she knows Tony’s story, no matter how certain she is at the outset that she does.

It should be obvious that we can’t know how strangers see things. But even with those you know well, imagining yourself in their shoes is harder than you might think. You may know many of the facts of their life, but understanding how they’ve made sense of those experiences requires deeper conversation.

Across issues of difference, such as race, ethnicity, gender, sexual orientation, ability or disability, religion, class, age, country or culture of origin, or neurodiversity, the challenge of understanding can be still greater. Not because people are different as human beings in some fundamental way, but because some of the differences in life experience may just be hard to appreciate or imagine. And the challenges are not symmetrical: those whose identities are aligned with the dominant culture typically underestimate the ways life is different for those who have been marginalized.

For example, when Viva, who is Latina and speaks English as a second language, lost her job, her friend Liz, who is White, can imagine some of what Viva is feeling, especially if Liz too has lost a job. But Liz might not be aware of the added stress Viva feels as she wonders whether bias or racism played a role, even as her former colleagues insist it didn’t. Or how this situation echoes her past experiences of discrimination or broader narratives of exclusion and racism throughout history.

On an episode of Marc Maron’s podcast in 2017, journalist Ta-Nehisi Coates elaborated on the challenge of understanding across differences when you are in the dominant group. Coates, who is Black and male, was discussing his own misperceptions regarding gender: “If you had said to me, ‘Sexual harassment is a huge problem in the workplace,’ I would have agreed, and said, ‘Yeah, that’s probably true.’…But [after MeToo], I didn’t know it was like this!…When you read the stories, then it’s like, ‘Ohhhh!’ I didn’t have that [sensitivity to it]. And how can you…? There are people who will never live in the world that you’re living in, ever.”

Curiosity, coupled with an awareness of some of the common mistakes we make in our attempts to understand others, is the best tool we have to help us begin to bridge our differences in experience and meaning-making.

What’s Your Story?

One way to shift your stance away from certainty is to get curious about what you don’t know about yourself. This may sound like an odd thing to worry about. After all, you’re with yourself all the time; wouldn’t you be pretty familiar with your own perspective?

In a word, no. The process by which we construct our stories about the world often happens so fast, and so automatically, that we are not even aware of all that influences our views. For example, when we saw what Jack was really thinking and feeling during his conversation with Michael, there was nothing about the heat being turned off, or about his daughter’s anger at canceling their dinner plans. Even Jack wasn’t fully aware of all the information behind his reactions.

And what implicit rules are important to him? Jack thinks to himself, “I can’t believe the way Michael treated me,” but he is unaware that this is based on an implicit rule of how people “should” treat each other. Jack’s rule is something like “You should always show appreciation to others no matter what.” Many of us agree with this rule, but it is not a truth, just a rule. Michael’s rule might be “Good friends can get angry with each other and not take it personally.” The point isn’t whose rule is better; the point is that they are different. But Jack won’t know they’re different unless he first considers what rules underlie his own story about what happened.

Recall the story of Andrew and his uncle Doug at the parade. We referred to Andrew as “truck obsessed.” This description is from his uncle’s point of view. Uncle Doug is aware of “how Andrew is,” but he is less aware of how he himself “is.” Andrew is truck obsessed if we use as the baseline his uncle Doug’s level of interest in trucks, which is zero. Among the four-year-old crowd, Andrew’s view is more likely the norm.

Embrace Both Stories: Adopt the “And Stance”

It can be awfully hard to stay curious about another person’s story when you have your own story to tell, especially if you’re thinking that only one story can really be right. After all, your story is so different from theirs, and makes so much sense to you. Part of the stress of staying curious can be relieved by adopting what we call the “And Stance.”

We usually assume that we must either accept or reject the other person’s story, and that if we accept theirs, we must abandon our own. But who’s right between Michael and Jack, Ollie and Thelma, or Darla and her boss, Carolina? Who’s right between a person who likes to sleep with the window open and another who prefers the window closed?

The answer is that the question makes no sense. Don’t choose between the stories; embrace both. That’s the And Stance.

The suggestion to embrace both stories can sound like advice to “pretend both of your stories are right.” But we mean something different. Don’t pretend anything. At least at first, don’t think of your task as determining whether what they are saying is right or wrong. Instead, think of your task as understanding them better — trying to make sense of the world as they see it. The act of understanding someone else’s story doesn’t require you to agree with it, or to give up your own. The And Stance allows you to recognize that how you each see things matters, that how you each feel matters. Regardless of what you end up doing, regardless of whether your story influences theirs or theirs yours, both stories matter.

The And Stance is based on the assumption that the world is complex, that you can feel hurt, angry, and wronged, and they can feel just as hurt, angry, and wronged. They can be doing their best, and you can think that it’s not good enough. You may have done something stupid, and they may have contributed to the problem as well. You can feel furious with them, and you can also feel love and appreciation for them.

The And Stance gives you a place from which to assert the full strength of your views and feelings without having to diminish the views and feelings of someone else. Likewise, you don’t need to give up anything to hear how someone else feels or sees things differently. Because you may have different information or different interpretations, or different things that matter most to each of you, both stories can make sense at the same time. For you, skydiving is about adventure and facing your fears. For your stepfather, it’s about your under-appreciation of the risks and your mother’s potential devastation if — God forbid — there’s an accident. It may be that as you share them, your stories change in response to new information or different perspectives. But they still may not end up the same, and that’s all right. Sometimes people have honest disagreements, but even so, the most useful question is not “Who’s right?” but “Now that we really understand each other, what’s a good way to manage this problem?”

Two Exceptions That Aren’t

You may be thinking that the advice to shift from certainty and arguing to curiosity and the And Stance generally makes sense, but that there must be exceptions. Let’s look at two important questions that may look like exceptions, but aren’t: (1) What about times when I absolutely know I’m right? and (2) Does the suggestion to “understand the other person’s story” apply even when I’m delivering bad news — for example, if I’m firing or breaking up with someone?

I Really Am Right

There’s an old story of two clerics arguing about how to do God’s work. In the spirit of conciliation, one finally says to the other, “You and I see things differently, and that’s okay. We don’t need to agree. You can do God’s work your way, and I’ll do God’s work His way.”

The tendency to think this way can be overwhelming. Even if you understand another person’s story with genuine insight and empathy, you may still stumble on the next step, thinking that however much their story makes sense to them, you are still “right” and they are still “wrong.”

For example, what about the conversation you have with your father about his smoking? You know you are right that smoking is bad for him, that the sooner he stops the better.

Fair enough. About each of those things, you are right. But here’s the rub: that’s not what the conversation is really about. It’s about how you each feel about your father’s habit, what he should do about it, and what role you should play. It’s about the terrible fear and sadness you feel as you imagine him becoming sick, and your frustration at feeling powerless to make him stop. It’s about his ambivalence, doing something that makes him feel good and at the same time truly worries him. It’s about his fear of how it would feel to try to stop and fail (again). The conversation is about many issues between the two of you that are complex and important to explore. It is not about the truth of whether smoking is bad for one’s health. Both of you already agree on that.

Even when it seems the dispute is about what’s true, you may find that being the one who’s right doesn’t get you very far. Your friend may deny that they are an alcoholic and that their drinking is affecting their family. But even if the whole world agrees with your assessment, asserting that you are right and trying to get them to admit it probably won’t help you help your friend.

What may help is to tell them about the impact their drinking has on you, and, further, to try to understand their story. What is keeping them in denial? What would it mean to them to admit they have a problem? What gets in the way? Until you understand their story, and share yours with them, you can’t help them find a way to rewrite the next chapter for the better. In this case, you may be right and your friend may be wrong, but merely being right doesn’t do you much good.[*]

Giving Bad News

What if you have to fire someone, end a relationship, or let a supplier know you’re cutting back on your order by 80 percent? In many difficult conversations, you don’t have the power to impose an outcome unilaterally. When firing someone or breaking up or reducing orders, you do. In such situations, it’s reasonable to wonder whether the other person’s story is still relevant.

Much of the difficulty in firing someone or in breaking up takes place in the Feelings and Identity Conversations, which we’ll explore in chapters 5 and 6. But the question of differing perspectives is also important. Remember, understanding the other person’s story doesn’t mean you have to agree with it, nor does it require you to give up your own. And the fact that you are willing to try to understand their view doesn’t diminish the power you have to implement your decision, and to be clear that your decision is final.

In fact, the And Stance is probably the most powerful place to stand when engaging in a difficult conversation that requires you to deliver or enforce bad news. If you are breaking up with someone, it allows you to say, “I’m breaking up with you because it’s the right thing for me [here’s why], and I understand how hurt you are, and that you think we should try again, and I’m not changing my mind, and I understand that you think I should have been more clear about my confusion earlier, and I don’t think that makes me a bad person, and I understand that I’ve done things that have hurt you, and I know you’ve done things that have hurt me, and I feel sad about this decision, and I’m still making it…. And, and, and.”

“And” helps you to be curious and clear.

To Move Forward, First Understand Where You Are

As you head down the path of improving how you deal with difficult conversations, you will notice that the question of how we each make sense of our worlds follows you like the moon in the night sky. It’s a beacon you can return to no matter where you are or with what difficult problem you are grappling.

Coming to understand the other person, and yourself, more deeply doesn’t mean that differences will disappear or that you won’t have to solve real problems and make real choices. It doesn’t mean that all views are equally valid or that it’s wrong to have strongly held beliefs. It will, however, help you evaluate whether your strong views make sense in light of new information and different interpretations, and it will help you help others to appreciate the power of those views.

Wherever you want to go, understanding — imagining yourself into the other person’s story — has got to be your first step. Before you can figure out how to move forward, you need to understand where you are.

The next two chapters delve more deeply into two problematic aspects of our stories — our tendency to misunderstand the intentions of others and discount our own impacts on them, and our tendency to focus on blame.

Skip Notes
* These are complicated questions, particularly as misinformation and disinformation proliferate. For more on discussing the “facts” see Question 1 on page 284. For thoughts on understanding people struggling with addiction or mental health, see Question 3 on page 298.

cNF

3

Don’t Assume They Meant It:

Disentangle Intent from Impact

Intentions are central to our story of what’s happening in a difficult situation, and strongly influence our judgments of others: if someone intended to hurt us, we judge them more harshly than if they hurt us by mistake. We’re willing to be inconvenienced by someone if they have a good reason; we’re irritated if we think they just don’t care about the impact of their actions on us. Though either blocks our way just as surely, we react differently to an ambulance double-parked on a narrow street than we do to a BMW.

The challenge is that the relationship between impact and intentions is often more complicated than it seems. When Dad misses his teenager’s soccer game, things seem pretty clear to the teen: “He knew it mattered, and still he didn’t come. He doesn’t care.” The teen knows the impact of Dad’s actions, but can’t know for sure what Dad’s intentions were.

As it happens, Dad wanted to come and intended to, but felt he had to respond immediately to a customer emergency or risk losing his job. So Dad made a tough choice. But if Dad insists that his good intentions should erase his teenager’s disappointment, he may compound his kid’s confusion and hurt: “You didn’t come to my game, and you don’t care how I feel about it.”

Managing intentions and impacts effectively requires careful attention to what we know and what we don’t, and a recognition that impact and intention don’t always go hand in hand.

The Battle Over Intentions

Consider the story of Leo and Trey, who have been in a relationship for two years. The couple was at dinner at the house of some friends. Leo was about to reach for a slice of cake, when Trey said, “C’mon, Leo, you’re going to regret that.” Leo, whose doctor recommended dietary changes after a recent health scare, shot Trey a nasty look, and the two avoided each other for a while. Later that evening things went from bad to worse:

Leo: I really resented it at dinner, the way you treated me in front of our friends.

Trey: The way I treated you? What are you talking about?

Leo: About the cake. You’re monitoring everything I eat. You have this need to control me.

Trey: Leo, I wasn’t trying to control you. I’m just trying to help you stick to your diet. You can get so defensive. You hear it as an attack on you, even when I’m trying to help.

Leo: Help?! Embarrassing me in front of our friends is your idea of helping? I’ve told you before that I don’t want to feel like you’re the food police.

Trey: You know, I just can’t win with you. If I say something, you think I’m controlling you, and if I don’t, you ask me why I let you eat things that make you sick. It’s so frustrating. Sometimes I wonder whether you start these fights on purpose.

This conversation left both Leo and Trey feeling frustrated, hurt, and misunderstood. What’s worse, it’s a dynamic they fall into repeatedly. Leo and Trey are engaged in a classic battle over intentions: Leo accuses Trey of trying to control him, and Trey denies it. Trey professes good intentions, which to Trey’s surprise, doesn’t help. They are caught in a cycle they don’t understand and don’t know how to break.

Two Key Mistakes

There is a way out. In the conversation above, there are two mistakes that make it more difficult than it needs to be — one by Leo and one by Trey.

When Leo says, “You have this need to control me,” he is talking about Trey’s intentions. Leo’s mistake is assuming he knows what Trey’s intentions are, when he doesn’t. It’s an easy — and derailing — mistake to make. And we do it all the time.

Trey’s mistake is to assume that once he clarifies that his intentions were good, Leo is no longer justified in being upset. Trey explains that he “wasn’t trying to control” Leo, that in fact, he was trying to help. And that, thinks Trey, should be the end of it. Good intentions clarified, problem solved. But the problem is not solved. Trey has overlooked the impact of his words on Leo. So now, in addition to feeling upset by Trey’s original comments, Leo also feels dismissed and unheard.

Fortunately, with some awareness, both of these common mistakes can be avoided.

The First Mistake: Assuming We Know Their Intentions

Exploring “Leo’s mistake” requires us to understand how our minds make sense of our interactions with others — specifically, the assumptions we make as we build our stories about what others intend. We’ll start this exploration of the mind with a look at a pair of circles.

We Leap to Intentions

In 1946 Austrian psychologist Fritz Heider ran a simple experiment. He made a short video of two circles — a small one and a larger one — moving across a screen. The circles moved steadily from left to right, with the smaller circle in front. Heider asked people to describe what they saw. Most responded with variations on: The big circle is chasing the little circle. Or: The little circle is running away.

That was the whole experiment.

Of course, the circles were neither chasing nor fleeing. They are just geometric shapes, with no backstory and no intentions. And that’s the point. Heider’s experiment highlights how automatically we turn what we perceive into a story. What we actually see is two circles moving across a screen. But our mind converts the circles into human-like characters, and these characters appear to us to have intentions or motivations. The big circle isn’t just moving, it’s “chasing.” It has a goal.

Why does our brain turn these shapes into characters with motivations? Neurologists and biologists have thoughts on this, but for our purposes, it’s enough to observe that our strong tendency to attribute intentions to things in our environment is part of our brain’s way of making sense of a potentially dangerous world. Is the person behind us chasing us? Do they mean us well or ill? From an evolutionary standpoint, these questions about the motivations of the people and animals nearby helped us survive.

But here’s the problem: while we care deeply about people’s intentions toward us, we don’t actually know what their intentions are. We can’t. Other people’s intentions are invisible to us. They exist only in their hearts and minds. However real and right our assumptions about other people’s intentions may seem to us, they are often incomplete or just plain wrong.

We Attribute Intentions Based on the Impact on Us

Much of the first mistake can be traced to one basic error: we make an attribution about another person’s intentions based on the impact of their actions on us. We feel hurt; therefore they intended to hurt us. We feel slighted; therefore they meant to slight us. Our thinking is so automatic that we aren’t even aware that our interpretation is only an assumption — or put another way, a guess. We are so taken in by our story that we can’t imagine how the other person could have intended anything else. Sometimes our guesses are right; often, they aren’t.

We Assume the Worst. Especially when we are frustrated, threatened, or in a relationship that has begun to fray, the conclusions we draw about others’ intentions are rarely charitable. When a teammate shows up late for practice (again), we don’t think, “I’ll bet something important delayed him.” More likely we think, “He just doesn’t care about wasting everyone’s time.” Is your supervisor hovering because she wants to be available to help, or because she has a need to micromanage? Your answer may depend in part on how the feedback makes you feel. When we feel hurt or frustrated by someone’s behavior, we assume the worst.

Of course, sometimes you feel hurt because someone meant to hurt you. The person you are dealing with is nasty, troubled, biased, or selfish. They are out to make you look bad or steal your best friend. So, our advice is not “Everyone has your best interests at heart” or “Always assume good intentions.” But even if they did mean to hurt you, there may be more to the story: perhaps they are responding to a bad impact you’ve had on them, or trying to protect themselves from what they fear you are about to do.

There are also examples of good impacts springing from dubious or mixed intentions. Beginning in the 1950s, for example, a nonprofit called Keep America Beautiful began encouraging Americans not to be “litter bugs.” That message had the beneficial impact of persuading most Americans that litter was a problem to which they should not contribute. But according to Heather Rogers in her book Gone Tomorrow: The Hidden Life of Garbage, this initiative was funded by industrial groups wanting, in part, to divert attention from industrial pollution and to pin the problems caused by the rise of nonreusable packaging on individual consumers.

The most empowered mindset is based on awareness: notice your attribution about the other person’s intentions; ask yourself what that attribution is based on; and remind yourself what you don’t know. Remember that, all things being equal, we tend to attribute intentions that are slightly more negative than is actually the case, especially when the impacts of their actions on us were negative. Don’t be a Pollyanna, but remember that your story about their intentions is at best an educated guess.

We Assume Bad Intentions Mean Bad Character. One danger of assuming the other person had bad intentions is that we too easily jump from “they had bad intentions” to “they are a bad person.” It’s not that our teammate came late to this practice; it’s that they lack discipline. Once we think we have someone figured out, we are prone to see all of their actions through that lens, and the stakes rise. Even if we never share our view with them, the impact remains. The worse our view of another’s character, the harder it is to be open and curious about who they really are and how they see things.

Technology Compounds the Challenge. With business and even personal relationships increasingly conducted via email, text messages, social media, video and conference calls, our guesses about what others intend become even more tenuous. When a customer emails, “I don’t suppose you’ve gotten to my order yet…,” is he meaning to be sarcastic? Is he annoyed? Or is he trying to tell you he knows you’re busy? Without tone of voice or body language to guide us, it’s hard to know.

We Treat Ourselves More Charitably. What’s ironic — and all too human — about our tendency to attribute bad intentions to others is how differently we treat ourselves. When your husband forgets to pick up the dry cleaning, he’s irresponsible. When you forget to book the airline tickets, it’s because you’re overworked and stressed out. When a coworker criticizes your work in front of department colleagues, she is trying to put you down. When you offer suggestions to others in the same meeting, you are trying to unlock the team’s creativity.

When we’re the ones acting, we know that most of the time we don’t intend to annoy, offend, or upstage others. We’re wrapped up in our own worries, and are often unaware that we’re having any negative impact on others. When we’re the ones acted upon, however, our story too easily slides into one about bad intentions and bad character.

Getting Their Intentions Wrong Is Costly

Our assumptions about other people’s intentions have a significant impact on our conversations, and on our relationships.

Accusing Them of Bad Intentions Creates Defensiveness. The easiest and most common way of expressing our assumptions about their intentions is with an accusation: “You’ll say anything to get elected.” “Does finding fault with me make you feel better about yourself?” “I’m never a priority for you.”

We think we are sharing our hurt, frustration, anger, or confusion. We are trying to begin a conversation that will end in greater understanding, perhaps some improved behavior, and maybe an apology. What they think we are doing is trying to malign them or pick a fight. (In other words, they make the same mistaken leap in judging our intentions.) And given how frequently our assumptions are incomplete or wrong, the other person often feels not just accused, but falsely accused. Few things are more aggravating.

We should not be surprised, then, that they try to defend themselves, or attack back. From their point of view, they are defending themselves from false accusations. From our point of view, they are just being defensive — we’re right, they just aren’t big enough to admit it. The result is a mess. No one learns anything and nothing changes.

Assuming Bad Intentions Can Make the Impact Worse. Our tendency to attribute negative intentions — and negative character — can have additional fallout: we may feel more upset about their actions.

Margaret’s experience illustrates this point. She had had her hip operated on by a prominent surgeon, a woman she found distant and hard to talk to. When Margaret hobbled in for her first appointment after surgery, the receptionist told her that the doctor had unexpectedly extended her European vacation. Exhausted and angry, Margaret imagined her doctor cavorting on some Greek Island, too self-important and inconsiderate to return on schedule. That image only compounded her indignance.

When Margaret finally saw the doctor a week later, she asked curtly how her vacation had been. The doctor responded that it had been wonderful. “I’ll bet,” Margaret said dryly. But the doctor went on: “It was a working vacation. I was helping set up and staff a hospital in a war zone in Ukraine. The conditions there are just horrendous.”

Learning what the doctor was really doing didn’t erase the inconvenience to Margaret. Yet knowing that the doctor was not acting out of selfishness, but from an unrelated and generous motivation, left Margaret feeling substantially better about having to wait the extra week.

Attributions Can Become Self-Fulfilling. Our assumptions about the other person’s intentions often come true, even when they aren’t true to begin with. You think your boss isn’t giving you enough responsibility. You assume that this is because she doesn’t trust you to do the work well. You feel demotivated by this state of affairs, figuring that nothing you do will change your boss’s mind. Your work suffers, and your boss, who hadn’t been concerned about your work before, is now quite worried. So she gives you even less responsibility than before.

When we think others have bad intentions toward us, it affects our behavior. And, in turn, how we behave affects how they treat us. Before we know it, our assumption that they have bad intentions toward us has come true.

It Becomes Harder to Turn Things Around. As attributions of bad intentions slide into a story of bad character, it becomes increasingly hard to repair a relationship. You grew up with a half-sister who paid little attention to you unless she wanted something. Her inattention was particularly painful during your tumultuous teenage years, and you coped by checking out of family life, complaining to friends about your self-absorbed and manipulative sibling. Now, years later, when your sister reaches out to you, you wonder what she really wants: Is she trying to show Mom that she’s the good one? Is she after the inheritance? In return, your skepticism and lack of responsiveness confirms for your sister the story she tells her friends: “My half-sister never really liked me, or accepted me as a part of the family.”

Could you come to appreciate who she is today? Maybe, but it will be difficult. As our attributions about intentions and character harden, we’re more likely to notice behavior that confirms our view, and to interpret ambiguous behavior in ways that align with how we’ve seen them all these years. Once we’ve got them figured out, we’ve got them boxed in. We become less open to the possibility of a more complex story, and even behaviors intended as a kind of peace offering can be dismissed (“Isn’t it just like her to try to seem like the ‘reasonable’ one?”).

We Aggravate Hostility — Especially Between Groups. We attribute intentions not just to individuals, but sometimes to entire groups — whether they are union members and management, ethnic groups that share a neighborhood, administrative staff and the professionals they support, or my family and your family.

A common and pernicious tendency is to treat groups as monolithic and to assume that all group members share a singular view and intention: management doesn’t care about employees; protesters just want attention; people who watch news on that network only want to hear what they already believe. But whether a group has two members or two million, each person is an individual. They may have certain important things in common, but it’s a mistake to attribute the intentions of one group member to all the members.

A number of years ago a small newspaper was experiencing racial strife among its workers. Black and Hispanic reporters complained about the absence of minority voices at the editorial level and threatened to organize a boycott unless practices were changed. In response, the executive editors met behind closed doors to consider what to do. No minority staffers were invited to the meeting. When the minority reporters learned of the meeting, they were outraged. The editors, for their part, asserted that they were meeting to figure out ways to be more inclusive of minority voices.

Members from each side met in facilitated sessions to try to resolve the conflict. At first, participants were saying things like, “Your side wants to intimidate us” and “You’ll do anything to hold onto power,” as if each person in that group had the exact same views and intentions.

Why is this a mistake? First, it simply doesn’t reflect the way the world is; people in any group have a range of views and intentions. Second, to the extent that we attribute negative intentions where they don’t exist, doing this with an entire group multiplies the mistake, and amplifies existing conflicts. When you think about groups, remind yourself that while group members may have many things in common and have broadly similar goals, it’s wrong to assume everyone in any group has the same motivations or views.

We Distract from the Heart of Our Message: The Impact. Perhaps the most self-defeating aspect of our tendency to assert that the other person had bad intentions is that it can divert the conversation away from what is often most important: the impact of their behavior on us. Rather than discussing that impact and what to do about it, we spend our time arguing over whether the other person really meant to have the impact they did. And this puts us in the awkward position of asserting that our guess about their intentions is more accurate than their own awareness of their intentions. Even if we “win” that argument, little changes, because the impact hasn’t been addressed.

Leo and Trey fall right into this pattern. Leo wants Trey to understand one thing: your comments upset me. But by tacking on an accusation about why Trey said what he said, Leo’s core concern — I felt upset — is lost in the shuffle.

The Second Mistake: Assuming Good Intentions Erase Bad Impact

Leo’s mistake of assuming he knows his husband’s intentions, though seemingly small, has big consequences. Trey’s error is just as costly. Ignoring what Leo has said about the impact of Trey’s behavior, Trey seeks to reassure Leo that he had good intentions: “I was just trying to help.” Trey assumes that because my intentions were good, Leo should not feel hurt. The thinking goes like this: “You said I meant to hurt you. I have now clarified that I didn’t. So you should now feel fine, and if you don’t, that’s your problem.”

This, of course, doesn’t follow. Well-intentioned people have bad impacts on one another all the time, and merely clarifying our intentions rarely causes the other person to “feel fine.” No matter how true, “I didn’t mean to get you fired” doesn’t fix our colleague’s jobless problem. When we assume that good intentions will fix the problem, we focus on clarifying those intentions, and end up ignoring the actual problem — the impact of our actions.

Protesting Our Good Intentions Can Be Costly

There are several reasons why jumping in to explain our good intentions can make matters worse, especially when we defend our intentions before really understanding how we’ve hurt the other person.

We Change the Topic of the Conversation. When Trey jumps in to explain what he intended, it actually changes the topic of the conversation — from Leo’s hurt to Trey’s good character. From what is important to them, to what is important to us.

This is particularly common — and damaging — in conversations about difference, power, or inequality. When an employee finally gets up the courage to raise concerns that promotion practices seem to be leaving behind colleagues of color, the rest of the conversation is often focused on the good intentions and efforts of management. That is a related but different topic. The employee wasn’t asking, “Is management well intentioned?” The employee wants to discuss the impact of the practices — that talented people are being overlooked. It’s not that discussing the intentions and practices of management is wrong, per se. But that discussion, if it’s relevant, should only happen after a thorough conversation about what the employee is seeing and experiencing.

We Leave Them Feeling Dismissed and Unheard. When we respond defensively, we are not only changing the topic from impacts to intentions and from them to us, we are also (usually unintentionally) dismissing their concerns. It’s as if someone says, “You set my hair on fire!” and the other person responds by saying, “But I’m a good person, right?”

Someone raising a concern is also implicitly asking: “Do you care about this issue?” “Do you care about me?” and “Can I trust you?” If we respond by immediately defending our intentions, we suggest that our answer is no. If an organizational climate survey reveals a lack of employee engagement, and management responds by explaining their good intentions, employees will start to wonder: If management isn’t curious about what’s producing the current climate, do they really care? Management’s responses begin to appear performative — check a box — rather than genuine. Problems persist, trust erodes, and claims of good intentions lose credibility.

As in the case of Margaret and her orthopedic surgeon, it does sometimes help to know that someone else’s behavior wasn’t malicious, or that they were, in fact, trying to help. But to the person who is hurt by our behavior, our intentions are of secondary concern at best. They want to talk about the hurt — the impact of our behavior — and they want to talk about it first. That’s what they’re raising, and that’s what they want you to hear and better understand. Until they feel heard, any effort to clarify your intentions is likely to sound like an excuse or avoidance.

We Ignore the Complexity of Human Motivations. Another problem with protesting our good intentions is that intentions are often more complex than just “good” or “bad.” Are Trey’s intentions purely supportive? Is Trey just trying to help Leo stick to healthy eating? Perhaps Trey is embarrassed by what Trey sees as Leo’s generally poor eating habits. At some level, Trey may want to control — or at least to influence — Leo’s decisions.

As is often the case, Trey’s intentions are probably mixed. Trey may not even be fully aware of all that is going on. But the answer to the question of what is truly motivating Trey is less important than Trey’s willingness to ask the question and look for an answer. If his first response to Leo is “No, I had good intentions,” then Trey’s message is: “I’m not willing to look at myself or learn from this conversation. I’m more interested in defending myself than I am in investigating the complexities of what might be going on for me in our relationship.”

Interestingly, when people take on the job of thinking hard about their intentions, it sends a positive message to the other person about the importance of the relationship. After all, you’d only do that kind of hard work for somebody you care about.

Sometimes Intentions Don’t Matter

We often run into trouble when we try to clarify our intentions too soon. But sometimes, it’s not a matter of timing; sometimes intentions just don’t matter. If a building is inaccessible to people in wheelchairs, it doesn’t matter that the architects didn’t intend to exclude people in wheelchairs. They didn’t have bad intentions — they just didn’t think about the issue. But what matters now is that the building is not accessible; what matters now is what we do about it. Likewise, if livestock contribute to the emissions that are driving climate change, we don’t need to discuss whether the people who first domesticated animals were hoping to raise sea levels. Obviously, they weren’t, but that doesn’t make the resulting problem any less urgent or catastrophic.

Avoiding the Two Mistakes

The good news is that both mistakes around intentions and impact are avoidable.

Avoiding the First Mistake: Disentangle Impact and Intent

How can Leo avoid the mistake of attributing intentions to Trey that Trey may not have? His first step is simply to recognize that there is a difference between the impact of Trey’s behavior on him and what Trey intended. He can’t get anywhere without disentangling the two.

Separating impact from intentions requires us to be aware of the automatic leap from “I was hurt” to “You intended to hurt me.” You can make this distinction by asking yourself three questions:

1. Actions: “What did the other person actually say or do?”

2. Impact: “What was the impact of this on me?”

3. Assumption: “Based on this impact, what assumption am I making about what the other person intended?”

Hold Your View as a Hypothesis. Once you have answered these three questions, the next step is to recognize that your assumption about their intentions is just a guess, a hypothesis.

Disentangle Impact and Intent

Aware of

Unaware of

My intentionsOther person’s intentions
Other person’s impact on meMy impact on other person

Your hypothesis is not based on nothing; you know what was said or done. But as we’ve seen, this is not a lot of evidence to go on. Your guess might be right and it might be wrong. In fact, your reaction might even say as much about you as it does about what they did. Perhaps you’ve had a past experience that gives their action special meaning to you. Many people find nicknames or teasing hostile, for example, because of bad experiences with siblings, while others think of teasing as a playful way to connect. Given the stakes, however, you can’t afford to level an accusation based on tenuous data.

Speak to the Impact; Inquire About Their Intentions. You can use your answers to the Action-Impact-Assumption path listed above to begin the difficult conversation itself: say what the other person did, tell them what its impact was on you, and, if relevant, note that you don’t know their intentions. Or you can explain your assumption about their intentions, as long as you are clear that it is a hypothesis that you are checking, not an accusation you are asserting is true.

Consider how this would change the beginning of the conversation between Leo and Trey. Instead of beginning with an accusation, Leo can begin by identifying what Trey said, and what the impact was on him:

Leo: You know when you said, “C’mon, Leo, you’re going to regret that”? I was embarrassed by that.

Trey: You were?

Leo: Yeah.

Trey: I was just trying to help you avoid sugar. I don’t see why you need to be upset about it.

Leo: Well, whether I should or shouldn’t be upset, I am. I felt embarrassed that you said it in front of our friends. Like I’m a child that’s getting slapped on the wrist, or needs a parent to tell them when they’re being “bad.” I’m not saying that you were doing it on purpose, but that’s the impact it had.

Trey: Wow. I didn’t realize it was so upsetting. I’m sorry. I don’t think I even noticed that others might hear. I’m confused about what it is you want me to say, if anything, if I see you going off your diet….

The conversation is only beginning, but it is off to a better start.

Don’t Pretend. Note that we are not suggesting you get rid of your assumptions about their intentions or pretend they don’t exist. That is neither realistic nor helpful. For example, the advice to “assume good intentions” is well meaning, but can have bad impacts. It can be heard as suggesting “If you see bad intentions, it’s not okay to say so” or “If you see bad intentions, it’s all in your head,” both of which can be damaging and inhibit candid conversation. Better simply to recognize that our assumptions are guesses subject to modification or disproof and to share them as such, then see what the other person has to say about it.

Recognize That Some Defensiveness Is Inevitable. No matter how skillfully you handle things, you are likely to encounter some defensiveness: despite Leo’s improved opening, Trey reacts here by telling Leo he shouldn’t feel the way he does. It’s best to anticipate a certain amount of self-justification, and to be prepared to clarify what you are trying to communicate, and what you are not.

The more you can relieve the other person of the need to defend themselves, the easier it becomes for them to take in what you are saying and to reflect on the complexity of their own motivations. For example, you might say, “I was surprised that you made that comment. It seemed uncharacteristic of you….” Assuming this is true (that it seemed uncharacteristic), you are giving some balance to the information you are bringing to their attention. If there was some malice mixed in with what they said, this balance makes it easier for them to admit.

Avoiding the Second Mistake: Listen for Feelings, and Reflect on Your Intentions

Now let’s turn to how to avoid the second mistake — rushing to explain our good intentions. This is a particularly tricky mistake to avoid, because it likely follows close on the heels of your counterpart making the first mistake. They raise something important and tack on an accusation about your intentions: “I don’t think you care about understanding me” or “You just want all the credit.” We feel falsely accused. Misunderstood. Maybe even self-righteous.

It’s natural to want to defend ourselves, our intentions, and our character. And yet, as we’ve seen, starting there leads to trouble.

Listen Past the Accusation for the Impact and Feelings. Remember that accusations about our bad intentions are always made up of two separate assertions: (1) we had bad intentions and (2) the other person was frustrated, hurt, or embarrassed, or there is a problem that needs addressing. Don’t pretend they aren’t saying the first. You may want to respond to it at some point. But neither should you ignore the second. And if you start by listening and acknowledging the impacts and feelings they are raising, it will make your conversation significantly easier and more constructive.

After all, the topic of this conversation from their point of view is their hurt, confusion, or concern. They may not even be aware that they’re suggesting bad intentions on your part. Instead of saying, “You didn’t include me,” they say, “You excluded me.” Just the verb they choose reveals an assumption that it was deliberate. They may not notice their own assumption, but we surely do.

Don’t take the bait. Note the assumption, set it aside, and first work to hear the hurt or anger, and the importance of their topic.

Be Open to Reflecting on the Complexity of Your Intentions. When it comes time to consider your intentions, avoid the temptation to say simply, “My intentions were pure.” We all have a tendency to think that about ourselves, and sometimes it’s true. But often, as we’ve seen, intentions are more complex.

We can imagine how the conversation might have gone if Trey followed this advice in his conversation with Leo:

Leo: I really resented it at dinner, the way you treated me in front of our friends.

Trey: The way I treated you? What do you mean?

Leo: About the cake. You’re monitoring everything I eat. You have this need to control me.

Trey: Wow. It sounds like what I said really hurt.

Leo: Of course it hurt. What did you expect? It was embarrassing!

Trey: Well, on the way over, you’d said you were going to be strict with yourself tonight and not have sugar. So I thought I could help you stick to that. But I can see how saying something in front of everyone might be embarrassing.

Leo: Especially since I’ve told you about my eating issues growing up.

Trey: Oh, Leo, I’m sorry. I kind of forgot about that. In the moment, I think I did want to stop you from eating the cake. Like it was my job to make sure you eat right. Plus, I knew how bad you would feel afterward.

Leo: So you were confused about how best to help.

Trey: Yes. What would you like me to do in that kind of situation?

Leo: I think in the moment, I’ll have to make my own choices. I don’t want to feel like my eating is being monitored, even if you’re just trying to do what’s best for me. If you’re concerned, we can discuss it afterward. And if I want advice or support in the moment, I can always ask for it.

Trey: That makes sense. And again, I’m sorry. I don’t like hurting you.

Leo: I know.

•   •   •

Understanding how we distort others’ intentions and dismiss our own impacts is crucial to untangling what happened between us. However, there’s still one more piece to the “What Happened?” Conversation that can get us into trouble — the question of who is to blame.

cU9

4

Abandon Blame:

Map the Contribution System

The ad agency you work for flies you to Colorado to pitch executives at ExtremeSport, a burgeoning sportswear company and a potentially important client. You begin your presentation, only to realize that you’ve got the wrong version of the slides. Shaken, you stumble through an unfocused pitch. With one slip, your assistant, who formatted and emailed you a set of slides that didn’t contain your most recent — and crucial — updates, has undermined weeks of hard work.

In Our Story, Blame Seems Clear

You blame your assistant, not just because he’s a convenient target for your frustration or because letting others know it was he and not you who screwed up may help salvage your reputation, but because it is the simple truth: this was his fault.

When you and your assistant finally discuss what went wrong, you can take one of two approaches. You can blame him explicitly, saying something like “I don’t know how you could have let this happen!” Or, if you tend to be less confrontational (or have been taught that blaming people isn’t helpful), you can blame him implicitly, with something less threatening, like “Let’s do better next time.” Either way, he’ll get the message: he’s to blame.

We’re Caught in Blame’s Web

Blame is a prominent issue in many difficult conversations. Whether on the surface or below, the conversation revolves around the question of who is to blame. Who is the bad person in this relationship? Who made the mistake? Who should apologize or be punished, and who gets to be righteously indignant?

But focusing on blame is a bad idea. Not because it’s hard to talk about. Nor because it can injure relationships and cause pain and anxiety. Many subjects are hard to discuss and have potentially negative side effects and are nonetheless important to address.

Focusing on blame is a bad idea because it inhibits our ability to learn what’s really causing the problem and to do anything meaningful to correct it. And because blame is often irrelevant and unfair. The urge to blame is based, quite literally, on a misunderstanding of what has given rise to the issues between you and the other person, and on the fear of being blamed. Too often, blaming also serves as a poor proxy for talking directly about hurt feelings.

That said, the advice “Don’t blame others” is no answer. You can’t move away from blame until you understand what blame is, what motivates us to want to blame each other, and how to move toward something else that will better serve your purposes in difficult conversations. That something else is the concept of contribution. The distinction between blame and contribution is not always easy to grasp, but it is essential to improving your ability to handle difficult conversations well.

Distinguish Blame from Contribution

At heart, blame is about judging and contribution is about understanding.

Blame Is About Judging, and Looks Backward

When we ask the question “Who is to blame?” we are really asking three questions in one. First, did this person cause the problem? Did your assistant’s actions (or inaction) cause you to have the wrong slides? Second, if so, how should his actions be judged against some standard of conduct? Was he incompetent, unreasonable, unethical? And third, if the judgment is negative, how should he be punished? Will he be yelled at? Warned? Perhaps even fired?

When we say, “This was your fault,” it is shorthand for giving condemning answers to all three questions. We mean not only that you caused this, but that you did something bad and should be punished. Moreover, we assume that blame is either-or. The question is which one of us is to blame, or mostly to blame. It’s no wonder that blame is such a loaded issue, and that we are quick to defend ourselves when we sense its approach.

When blame is in play, you can expect defensiveness, strong emotion, interruptions, and arguments about what “good assistants,” “loving spouses,” or “any reasonable person” should or shouldn’t do. When we blame someone, we are offering them the role of “the accused,” so they do what accused people do: they defend themselves any way they can. Given what’s at stake, it’s easy to see why the dance of mutual finger-pointing often turns nasty.

Contribution Is About Understanding, and Looks Forward

Contribution asks a related but different set of questions. The first question is “How did we each contribute to bringing about the current situation?” Or put another way: “What did we each do or not do to get ourselves into this mess?” The second question is, “Having identified the contribution system, how can we change it — what can we each do about it as we go forward?” In short, contribution is useful when our goal is to understand what actually happened so that we can improve how we work together in the future. In the worlds of both work and personal relationships, too often we deal in blame when our real goals are understanding and change.

To illustrate, let’s return to the ExtremeSport story and imagine two contrasting conversations between you and your assistant. The first conversation focuses on blame, the second on contribution.

You: I wanted to talk to you about my presentation at ExtremeSport. The file you sent was labeled “Final,” but it was an earlier draft that didn’t contain several of the most important additions I’d made. The situation was unbelievably awkward, and made me look terrible. We simply can’t work this way.

Assistant: I heard. I’m so sorry. I just, well, you probably don’t want to hear my excuses.

You: I just don’t understand how you could let this happen.

Assistant: I’m really sorry.

You: I know you didn’t do it on purpose, and I know you feel bad, but I don’t want this to happen again. You understand what I’m saying?

Assistant: It won’t. I promise you.

All three elements of blame are present: you caused this, I’m judging you negatively, and implicit in what I am saying is that one way or another you will be punished, especially if it happens again.

In contrast, a conversation about contribution might sound like this:

You: I wanted to talk to you about my presentation at ExtremeSport. Just as I began, I realized the slides you’d formatted and sent me weren’t the final ones.

Assistant: I heard. I’m so sorry. I feel terrible.

You: I appreciate that. I’m feeling bad too. Let’s retrace our steps and think about how this happened. I suspect we may each have contributed to the problem. From your point of view, did I do anything differently this time?

Assistant: I’m not sure. We were working on three accounts at once, and on the one just before this one, when I asked if I was working from the right version, you seemed agitated. I know it is my responsibility to know which is the latest version, but sometimes when we’re emailing back and forth, and uploading multiple versions for multiple clients under tight deadlines, it can get confusing.

You: If you’re unsure, you should always ask. But it sounds like you’re saying I don’t always make it easy to do that.

Assistant: Well, I do feel intimidated sometimes. When you get really busy, it’s like you don’t want to be bothered. The day you left you were in that kind of mood. I was trying to stay out of your way, because I didn’t want to add to your frustration. At one point, I think I was working on one version and you were making changes to a different one, and later I must have confused them. I should have double- and triple-checked, but didn’t. I know that you usually double-check the final slides beforehand, and I didn’t hear anything, so I figured it was okay.

You: Yeah, I do usually double-check, but that day I just didn’t have time. I think we’d both better double-check every time. And I do get in those moods. I know it can be hard to interact with me when I’m like that. I need to work on being less stressed and abrupt. But if you’re unsure, I need you to ask questions no matter what kind of mood I’m in.

Assistant: So you want me to ask questions even if I think it will annoy you?

You: Yes, and I’ll try to be less irritable. Can you do that?

Assistant: Well, talking about it like this makes it easier. I realize it’s important.

You: You can even refer to this conversation. You can say, “I know you’re under pressure, but you made me promise I’d ask this….” Or just say, “Hey, you promised not to be such a jerk!”

Assistant: [laughs] Okay, that works for me.

You: And let’s also work through how we can more effectively manage version control issues so there’s less confusion to begin with….

In the second conversation, you and your assistant have begun to identify the contributions that you each brought to the problem, and the ways in which each of your reactions are part of an overall pattern: you feel anxious and distracted about an upcoming presentation, and snap at your assistant. He assumes you want him out of your way, and withdraws. Something falls through the cracks, and then you are even more annoyed and worried the next time you are preparing, since you’re no longer sure you can trust your assistant to have your back. So you become more abrupt, increasingly unapproachable, and the communication between you continues to erode. Mistakes multiply.

As you get a handle on the interactive contribution system the two of you have created, you can see what you each need to do to avoid or alter that system in the future. As a result, this second conversation is much more likely than the first to produce lasting change in the way you work together. Indeed, the first conversation runs the risk of reinforcing the problem. Since part of the system is that your assistant feels apprehensive about talking to you because he fears provoking your anger, a conversation about blame is likely to make that tendency worse, not better. If you go that way, he’ll eventually conclude that you’re impossible to work with, and you’ll report that he’s incompetent.

Contribution Is Joint and Interactive

Focusing on the contributions of both the boss and the assistant — seeking understanding rather than judgment — is critical. This is not just good practice, it accords more closely with reality. Far more often than not, when things go wrong in human relationships, everyone has contributed in some way.

Of course, this is not how we usually experience contribution. A common distortion is to see contribution as singular — that what has gone wrong is either entirely our fault or (more often) entirely theirs.

Only in a B movie is it that simple. In real life causation is almost always more complex. A contribution system is present, and that system includes inputs from both people. Think about a baseball pitcher facing a batter, taking signals from the catcher. Each makes choices that contribute to the outcome of the pitch. If the batter strikes out, he might explain that he wasn’t seeing well, that his wrist injury was still bothering him, or perhaps that he simply failed to come through in the clutch. The catcher might describe the strikeout by saying, “I knew he was thinking curve, so I called for a high fastball,” while the pitcher might say, “I was in a zone. I knew I had him before he even got in the batter’s box.”

Who is right, the batter, the catcher or the pitcher? Of course, the answer is all three, at least in part. Whether the batter strikes out or hits a home run is a result of the interaction between the catcher, the pitcher, and the batter. The actions of each of them are required for the outcome.

It’s the same in difficult conversations. Aside from exceptional contexts such as child abuse, most situations that give rise to a conversation at work or in everyday life are the result of a joint contribution system. Focusing on only one of the contributors obscures rather than illuminates that system.

The Costs of the Blame Frame

There are situations where focusing on blame is intentional. Most legal systems are set up to apportion blame, both in the criminal and civil courts. The rationale is that assigning blame publicly, against clearly articulated legal or moral standards, tells people what is expected of them, protects potential victims, and helps society to exercise justice. Some critique these rationales in theory and many people question the effectiveness and fairness of their implementation, but most people believe that bad behavior should have consequences and that therefore blame has an important social function.

When Blame Is the Goal, Understanding Is the Casualty

But even in situations that call for a clear assignment of blame, there is a cost. Once the specter of punishment — legal or otherwise — is raised, learning the truth about what happened becomes more difficult. People are understandably less forthcoming, less open, less willing to apologize. After a car accident, for example, an automaker expecting to be sued may resist making safety improvements for fear it will seem an admission that the company should have done something before the accident.

“Truth commissions” often are created because of this trade-off between assigning blame and gaining an understanding of what really happened. A truth commission offers clemency in return for honesty. In South Africa, for example, it is unlikely that so much would now be known about past abuses under the apartheid system if criminal investigations and trials had been the only means of discovery.

Focusing on Blame Hinders Problem-Solving

When the dog disappears, who’s to blame? The person who opened the gate or the one who failed to grab her collar? Should we argue about that or look for the dog? When the tub overflows and ruins the living room ceiling below, should we blame the forgetful bather? The spouse who called the bather downstairs? The manufacturer who designed an overflow drain that is too small? The plumber who failed to mention it? The answer to who contributed to the problem is all of the above. When your real goal is finding the dog, fixing the ceiling, and preventing such incidents in the future, focusing on blame is a waste of time. It neither helps you understand the problem looking back, nor helps you fix it going forward.

Blame Can Leave a Bad System Undiscovered

Even if punishment seems appropriate, using it as a substitute for really figuring out what went wrong and why is a disaster. The VP of Commodity Corp. championed the decision to build a new manufacturing plant as a way to increase profits. However, not only did the plant fail to increase profits, but the resulting increase in market supply actually brought profits down. At the time of the original decision to build the plant, several people privately predicted this, but didn’t speak up.

To address the situation, the VP was fired and a new strategic planner was brought on board. By removing the person who made the bad decision and replacing him with someone “better,” it was assumed that the management issue was now fixed. But while the company had changed one “part” in the contribution system, it had failed to look at the system as a whole. Why did those who predicted failure keep silent? Were there implicit incentives that encouraged expansion or risk-taking? What structures, policies, and processes continue to allow poor decisions, and what would it take to change them?

This dynamic is all too common. A video surfaces of police misconduct; the department says such behavior is an exception and against policy, and to “prove” it, fires the “bad apples.” But incidents recur. A story emerges of bullying in school. The perpetrators are suspended, but nothing else changes, even as students say it’s a common pattern.

Removing one player in a system is sometimes warranted. But if the goal is to create real change, it should not be a substitute for the hard work of examining the larger contribution system.

The Benefits of Understanding Contribution

Fundamentally, using the blame frame makes conversations more difficult, while seeking to understand the contribution system makes a difficult conversation easier and more likely to be productive.

Contribution Is Easier to Raise
