Coach: That’s an important issue. See if you can bring it up as an issue of joint contribution rather than blame.

Jack: Michael, there’s another issue I want to raise. I get the sense that you’re thinking the fact that the earnings chart was off was entirely my responsibility.

Michael: Jack, we don’t need to get into that again. I’m not trying to beat you over the head with it. I understand that you worked hard on the prospectus design and I appreciate that.

Jack: I know. I just want to offer a different angle on this blame question. Your reaction was that since I did the work, the chart problem was my fault. And my reaction was that since you reviewed the proof and gave the go-ahead, that you had contributed to this problem as well —

Michael: No, I never said I proofread it. That was your job. What I indicated was that assuming there were no mistakes, it was okay for you to print.

Jack: That’s just my point. I’m saying that we both had a hand in the problem. We misunderstood each other. I’m not saying there’s a right or wrong. If we had each understood the other more clearly, we would have been less likely to have gotten into the mess we did.

Michael: That’s certainly true. But so what?

Jack: The point is, we are more likely to avoid this kind of problem in the future if we are more careful to communicate clearly. I should have asked you point-blank whether you had proofread the prospectus carefully, and you might have said more clearly that you had not. Either one of those would have been useful, and would be useful for us next time.

Michael: I think that makes sense.

Jack: Wow. That was much easier to discuss than blame, and much more helpful.

Coach: And notice that talking about contribution focuses you naturally on problem-solving. Let’s work a bit more on that. You each have your own view on whether the chart should have been redone. Do a little problem-solving on that issue.

Step Five: Problem-Solving

Jack: Michael, let’s think about how we should handle a difference of judgment if it happens again in the future. For example, whether the prospectus needs to be revised and reprinted.

Michael: I think as the client in this situation, we should just do it my way. I don’t see it as some sort of joint decision.

Jack: I agree, in terms of the final decision. You should make it in that case. I guess I’m wondering how to give you the benefit of my judgment before the decision is made. I can imagine there will be times when you have a certain view, and then we talk about it, and you change your mind.

Michael: That’s true. So maybe if we’re more clear about what the purpose of the conversation is, then instead of thinking you’re trying to make the final decision, I’ll know that you’re just giving me your opinion.

Jack: That makes sense.

Michael: But sometimes I don’t have time to have a long conversation about it.

Jack: I understand that. It would help me if you would tell me that. Otherwise I don’t understand why you’re getting so frustrated in the conversation.

Michael: So I can just say, “I don’t have time to talk about it”?

Jack: Yes, and also tell me why. That you’ve got to get something out by noon, or that this earnings issue is a touchy one, or that we can talk about it later. It’ll only take five seconds, and it’ll save me from getting frustrated with you for not listening.

Michael: I can see why that would be frustrating.

Coach: Jack, you and Michael are on your way. Nice job!

Jack: As long as I’m on a roll, I want to bring up with Michael the thing that in some ways is the most difficult, and that’s the question of our friendship. I want to make sure that none of this hurt our friendship.

Coach: Check your purposes on that. “Make sure that none of this hurt our friendship” sounds like you’re going to put words in his mouth. It’s a little controlling. If you’re going to ask a question, make sure it’s an open question. Just ask him how he’s feeling about your friendship. If the problem did hurt your friendship, you want him to be open about saying so.

Jack: I’m glad we’re working through these issues. I think it’s hard to work with friends. I guess I wonder whether you think this has affected our friendship.

Michael: Well, what’s your answer to that question?

Jack: Honestly? Now that we’ve talked it through I feel much better about things. Before we talked I was pretty angry. And probably a little hurt. If we hadn’t discussed this at some point, it would have been easy for me to figure we were not going to stay friends.

Michael: I’m surprised by that. You and I certainly react differently to this kind of thing. I was not happy with our working relationship, but I thought our friendship was fine. I view them as separate. But since you obviously think about it differently, I’m glad we talked about it.

Jack: Looks like we’re friends again!

Coach: You handled it skillfully.

Jack: Thanks. I suspect we won’t have these sorts of problems in the future.

Coach: I don’t know about that. In fact, I think you are better off assuming that you will. Now, though, you know that it’s okay to talk about them, so the misunderstandings may not be as emotionally draining and are less likely to threaten the relationship. But is this the last difficult conversation you’ll have with Michael? I doubt it.

As the saying goes, “Life is just one damn thing after another.” It is, of course. And now you have a few more skills to handle it.

A Difficult Conversations Checklist

Step 1: Prepare by Walking Through the Three Conversations

1. Sort out What Happened.

Where does your story come from (information, past experiences, rules)? Theirs?

What impact has this situation had on you? What might their intentions have been? What impact might you have had on them? What were your intentions?

What have you each contributed to the problem?

2. Understand Emotions.

Explore your emotional footprint, and the bundle of emotions you experience. What might the other person be feeling?

3. Ground your Identity.

What’s at stake for you about you? What do you need to accept to be better grounded? What identity issues might be coming up for them?

Step 2: Check Your Purposes and Decide Whether to Raise the Issue

Purposes: What do you hope to accomplish by having this conversation? Shift your stance to support learning, sharing, and problem-solving.

Deciding: Is this the best way to address the issue and achieve your purposes? Is the issue really embedded in your Identity Conversation? Can you affect the problem by changing your contributions? If you don’t raise it, what can you do to help yourself let go?

Step 3: Start from the Third Story

1. Describe the problem as the difference between your stories. Include both viewpoints as a legitimate part of the discussion.

2. Share your purposes.

3. Invite them to join you as a partner in sorting out the situation together.

Step 4: Explore Their Story and Yours

Listen to understand their perspective on what happened. Ask questions. Acknowledge the feelings behind the arguments and accusations. Paraphrase to see if you’ve got it. Try to unravel how the two of you got to this place.

Share your own viewpoint, your past experiences, intentions, feelings.

Reframe, reframe, reframe to keep on track. From truth to perceptions, blame to contribution, accusations to feelings, and so on.

Step 5: Problem-Solve

Once you’ve understood what each person cares about and why:

Invent options that meet each side’s most important concerns and interests.

Look to standards for what should happen. Keep in mind the standard of mutual caretaking; relationships that always go one way rarely last.

Talk about how to keep communication open as you go forward.

c2B4

Ten Questions People Ask About Difficult Conversations

c2BC

Ten Questions People Ask About Difficult Conversations

1. It sounds like you’re saying everything is relative. Aren’t some things just true, and can’t someone simply be wrong?

2. What if the other person really does have bad intentions — lying, bullying, or intentionally derailing the conversation to get what they want?

3. What if the other person is particularly hard for me to deal with?

4. What if the other side has more power, or the game is stacked against me?

5. If I’m the boss/parent, can’t I just tell my subordinates/children what to do?

6. Isn’t this a very American approach? How does it work in other cultures?

7. What about conversations that aren’t face-to-face? What should I do differently if I’m on the phone, text, email, video call, or social media?

8. Why do you advise people to “bring feelings into the workplace”? I’m not a therapist, and shouldn’t business decisions be made on the merits?

9. Who has time for all this in the real world?

10. My Identity Conversation keeps getting stuck in either-or: I’m perfect or I’m horrible. I can’t seem to get past that. What can I do?

1. It sounds like you’re saying everything is relative. Aren’t some things just true, and can’t someone simply be wrong?

Some people who read Difficult Conversations wonder if we’re arguing that facts are irrelevant or that all views are equally reasonable. These questions arise both in practical discussions (“We should close the Newark plant”; “I should be first author”; “Jasper should be grounded for a month”) and in more profound ones about values and beliefs (“Health care is a human right”; “Abortion is murder”; “What matters is equality of outcome, not equality of opportunity”; “My God is the one true God”). Such questions about the role of facts and truth take on added urgency with the proliferation of misinformation (that the purveyor believes is true but is false) and disinformation (that the purveyor spreads because it is false). How, as a society, we come to grips with these crucial challenges will impact the future of democracy in the United States and beyond, as well as the health of the planet.

We don’t have all the answers to these challenges, but below we share a few observations and distinctions we hope will be clarifying and helpful.

Facts Aren’t Relative, Although They Can Be Hard to Pin Down

Facts exist, and people can be right or wrong about them. Let’s start with a simple everyday example. If the dinner bill is $30, and you conclude that a 15 percent tip is $6.00, you are wrong; 15 percent is $4.50. But if you think 15 percent is too little and that 20 percent is the “correct tip,” that’s a judgment, not a fact — even if it is based on a survey showing that 20 percent is the usual and customary tip for this service in this location. Those are facts, but they don’t necessarily make 20 percent the “correct” tip amount.

To make conversations productive, especially in a context of strong emotion, high stakes, and complex perceptions, a critical first step is to distinguish clearly between facts on the one hand, and opinions, assumptions, values, interests, predictions, and judgments on the other. That your five-year-old threw her dinner on the floor is a fact; whether and how she should be disciplined are judgments. The number of minutes you spoke during the meeting is a fact; whether you “inappropriately dominated the meeting” is a judgment. That hundreds of thousands of people were murdered in the Rwandan genocide is a fact; whether the United States should have intervened is a matter of interests, values, and assumptions.

Facts can be clarified, verified, and measured, though sometimes even facts are hard to pin down. Watch any courtroom drama to see examples. A video that has been entered into evidence appears to reveal that no one was present at the time in question, but is its time stamp accurate? Has the video been edited? There are factual answers to these questions, but we may have difficulty determining what they are.

Moreover, when memory is a factor, the level of uncertainty increases dramatically. Studies show that people are, on the whole, not very reliable witnesses, even when they are paying attention. We often feel certain about memories that are in fact erroneous. We can even transform memories unconsciously, misplacing events in time and location and misremembering who was present, even when the memory itself remains vividly specific. Brain scientists are beginning to understand how this process works neurologically and to confirm that it is not uncommon. Some of the latest research, for example, has found that each time you recollect or retell a memory, you actually rewrite it as you put it back into storage. Even as little as twenty-four hours later, your recollection of what happened may be version 18, depending on how much you’ve been dwelling on it.

Complexity presents another challenge. How do you know that Mount Everest is the world’s highest peak? You are unlikely to have made measurements and calculations yourself. If you believe that Mount Everest is the highest peak, it’s because you trust those who have told you it is. And, of course, it couldn’t be any other way. We have to rely on other sources for much of what we “know.”

So when two people disagree, part of the explanation may be differences in information, or around what facts are thought to mean, or differences of interests and values. But the disagreement could also be about whose assertion of facts we trust. It’s not a coincidence that we often agree with the “facts” as put forth by our preferred news outlet or politician. We believe them, not because we have independently assessed the quality of their evidence; we believe them because we already trust them. And sometimes this is, in part, because they are telling us what we want to hear.

A further complication is that we humans are capable of believing something is true even when most or all of the evidence says it’s not. This requires a kind of divided mindset that we are capable of inhabiting, similar to how a third-grader might think about Santa Claus: “Based on what I know about the world, it’s impossible to conclude that Santa Claus exists. But I’m so happy he does!” To a greater or lesser extent, we tend to believe what we want to believe.

As the world grows more complex and social media gives each of us the ability to broadcast, there is more opportunity for disinformation to proliferate. The traditional referees who judged the quality of information and served as gatekeepers have been sidelined, and we’ve yet to figure out an effective, crowd-sourced replacement. And in the battle between truth and disinformation, disinformation has at least one advantage: it doesn’t need to tell a provably true story, it just needs to cast doubt on our current story.

Tobacco companies created the template. Unable to prove that smoking was safe, they made often distorted or spurious arguments against the studies showing its harm, correctly predicting that many smokers would use this as an excuse not to quit: “Until the experts get this sorted out for sure, I’m going to keep smoking.” But all scientific information involves some doubt. No study is perfect, and conclusions sometimes shift over time as we learn more. The question, as in a court, is where does the preponderance of the evidence lie? The answer to that question has been unambiguous for decades: smoking kills. Another example is climate: the great preponderance of the evidence concludes that rising levels of carbon dioxide in the atmosphere are warming the planet, but some argue that we should not act until any remaining uncertainties are resolved.

So when you yourself are trying to assess what is true, try to weigh the evidence as best you can on both sides. Even if you don’t fully understand all the evidence, you can certainly notice if none is offered, or if most of the evidence points one way. And remember, a mere assertion that something is true by your favorite politician or celebrity is not evidence. The assertion that they have evidence (that they won’t show you) is also not evidence. Anyone can assert anything — “I’m telling you, if you ingest these magic beans I’m selling, you’ll live forever.” You need to ask what the assertion is based on, and assess the quality of the answer as best you can.

What are the implications for how we to talk to each other?

First, let’s acknowledge the frustration: it’s difficult to talk with someone you strongly believe is factually (and stubbornly) wrong about something important, whether it’s due to ignorance, denial, motivated reasoning, magical thinking, misplaced trust, or the sheer complexity of the topic. What to do?

Start by asking yourself why you believe what you believe. Have you studied the topic? Read books, watched documentaries? Experienced things firsthand? Heard it on the news, read about it on social media? The clearer you are about why you think what you think, and why that evidence persuades you, the better you will be able to explain to others both the significance and limitations of your views.

Next, check your purposes: trying to change their mind is likely to generate resistance and inhibit listening; it’s also unlikely to happen in one conversation on a complex topic. A better purpose is mutual understanding, and short of that, at least increasing your understanding of the other person. Where there is disagreement, it is imperative to find out what the other is seeing, how they make sense of it, and where and why the two of you disagree. After demonstrating an understanding of their view, you can ask how they factor in the key considerations that lead you to a different view. As we’ve said, the more open you are to learning about their view, the more likely it is that there will eventually be movement in their view.

Not All Stories Are Equal, but It Takes a Learning Conversation to Find Out

The need to understand the basis of the other person’s story — and here we are talking about any conversation, not just conversations with political implications — intensifies when the disagreement is about interpretations and judgments, about what the facts mean. And this is another domain where the question of relativity arises: “Are you saying that his interpretation is just as valid as mine? Because it isn’t!” We all feel this sometimes. “I understand where his expectation comes from; I just don’t think it’s reasonable. I think it’s a reflection of his ‘issues’ and neuroses, not of what’s fair.”

We are not saying that all interpretations and stories are equal. Some interpretations are more reasonable than others, or at least are likely to seem so to most people. There are a number of reasons for this. Some stories reflect a more complete understanding of a given situation — that is, they take account of more of the available information. Others rely on fewer or less extreme assumptions, or on assumptions that are more closely tied in time or place to the situation at hand. Still others have fewer logical leaps or internal contradictions.

But again, to be able to compare stories by such standards you first have to engage in a learning conversation to explore each story, where it comes from and what it’s based on, and how the two stories intersect. This is equally true whether you are trying to persuade an adversary, a teammate, or other observing constituents.

The bottom line? When you think their view is “just plain wrong,” you could be right, but take a moment to reexamine your assumptions. There’s always a chance that they know something you don’t, and there’s no downside to testing your own view and seeking to understand theirs. Sometimes multiple interpretations of a situation may all make sense, as with the famous ambiguous picture that can be seen as either an old woman or a young one. So inquire into their view looking for the sense rather than the nonsense in it. Paraphrase it back, share where and why you see it differently, and ask them for their reactions. Look for different information, different interpretations of ambiguous information, or different assumptions about missing information that help explain your differing views.

At the end of the day, your counterpart may simply not be persuaded. However robust your learning conversation, they may choose not to acknowledge what you or others see, and you will need to assess at what point you are better off moving on to your no-agreement alternative, or other strategies of influence. But most of us give up too soon, before we’ve really understood and had a fair chance to weigh the integrity of the other’s story.

One thing it may help to ask before giving up is what they would have to learn to persuade them to change or reconsider their view. Even if they say that nothing would persuade them, you gain valuable information: you learn that any further attempt to persuade them may be a waste of effort. On the other hand, if their answer is more nuanced, you’ve identified the challenge and can assess your ability to meet it. (By the way, it’s also worth asking yourself what you would have to learn to change your view.)

Whether or Not Some Truths Are Absolute, as Human Beings Our Ability to Perceive Such Truths Is Limited

Finally we come to the question of dealing with people — ourselves or others — who hold particular beliefs as certain, absolute truths. These may be people with religious views based on a sacred source, such as the Bible, Torah, or Quran. Or they may be people who reject any such faith-based beliefs and insist only on the primacy of observable, measurable facts and evidence.

Our own beliefs notwithstanding, the question for us is how to talk about or across such views productively. While it may seem more difficult in this context, our answer is the same as for any learning conversation: be respectful, and seek to understand the sense in how others see it. You may have something to learn, and understanding their story helps you know how best to help them learn.

When we ourselves hold beliefs as absolute truth, we tend to think, “surely this should be an exception to that general rule of mutual understanding. If my beliefs represent the truth, isn’t it just a question of helping them see it?” In a word, no. We have no power to make other people see something as we do, and our urgent attempts at one-way persuasion usually result in increased resistance rather than greater understanding.

The problem, of course, is that people disagree about what the truth is. However certain we feel, others with different views may feel just as sure. Even in a community that believes wholeheartedly in certain absolute truths, there may still be disagreement on the implications and meaning of those truths. Our own experience facilitating a discussion of these issues at a seminary allowed us to witness a deep and complex appreciation of the challenges of being a moral leader while maintaining appropriate humility about the ability of any human to perceive the mind of God.[*1]

In this context, the critical question is less whether there is absolute truth than whether and how well we can perceive it. Perhaps the only thing a human being can be truly sure of is that one can’t be completely sure. That is the realm of God, even if you don’t believe in God.

This doesn’t mean we can’t argue with passion and conviction over issues about which we care deeply. But when we do so, we should avoid hubris and maintain some humility and respect. After all, even our own views sometimes change with time.

We Can Do Better

When we first wrote the answer to this question in the spring of 2010, our corner of the world, and many others, faced a challenging set of social, political, religious, and moral divides — including the appropriate size and role of government, health care reform, education reform, abortion, marriage equality, immigration policy, homeland security, climate and energy policy, social justice, and the economy. There was at least a perception that the gap between people of divergent views was growing. Anger and even outrage were prevalent on each side of each divide. If we are righteous, we assumed, it is because there is so much at stake, so much to fear, and because we are so tired of the other side’s not listening and not caring. We were sick of the corruption, the lying, the stupidity, the self-interested nonsense that passes for informed opinion and public policy.

Now, as we revise the book in the spring of 2023, these challenges, if anything, have deepened.

Where do the authors of this book come down on all of this? Our various views on the substance of these debates are not relevant. But we have strong views on the process. As divides grow larger and more passionate, and disinformation more dangerous, good communication becomes more difficult and more important. With a passionate viewpoint comes the responsibility to be informed about the issues involved and to listen to how people with a different point of view see them. Not necessarily with the goal of agreeing or even of finding common ground. But at least with the goal of understanding how your neighbor sees it. Not the media, not the internet or the blogosphere, not bumper stickers and placards, but your neighbor, whether across the street, across the aisle, or across the world.

Consider this assertion: the more passionate we are about the issues that matter most to us, the more likely we are to have a cartoonish view of those who see things differently. That statement may infuriate you. You may find yourself chafing against such a ridiculous generalization. But flip it around: When others think your view is self-interested or shallow, base, and maybe even evil, do you think they see you clearly? Is what they’ve heard and read an accurate portrayal of what you see and feel? No. They’ve turned you into a cartoon they can dismiss without having to confront the fact that you care as much as they do, that you are a person of principle and conviction, that you’re working hard to do what’s right in the face of the very same human limitations and frailties we all confront.

Most of the time, they are too.[*2]

2. What if the other person really does have bad intentions — lying, bullying, or intentionally derailing the conversation to get what they want?

We can’t know for sure what is motivating another person. What feels deliberate and strategically intentional to us may be a hotheaded emotional reaction in response to a trigger we’re unaware of, or an unthinking response by someone at the limits of their ability to stay constructive. Surprisingly often, an “obvious” and self-serving lie turns out to be a person’s actual belief.

Nevertheless, people do lie and sometimes have quite bad intentions toward us. And certainly there are situations where people —intentionally or otherwise — manipulate, threaten, delay, obfuscate, bully, or derail in an attempt to get their way.

In these situations we have three initial pieces of advice. First, be careful about rewarding bad behavior. If you give up and hand people what they want simply “to avoid the hassle,” you end up teaching them that behaving badly pays off, and you will soon see more of the same.

Second, beware of reacting in kind or “playing their game.” Remember that your behavior can affect your reputation far beyond this one interaction. So even if you are being lied to, it will rarely serve you to lie in return and undercut your reputation for integrity.

Note that being trustworthy is not the same as being trusting. If they haven’t earned your trust, you have no obligation to offer it. If they “dare” you, remember the “And Stance”: “Don’t you trust me?” “Actually, I don’t know you well enough to be sure, and if you are telling the truth I assume you have no problem offering verification or a guarantee.” Rather than simply reacting in kind, focus on your objective and how to move toward it.

Third, seek to understand why they think their intentions and actions are justified. We tend to ascribe bad behavior to bad character: they lie because they are bad people. Unfortunately, if we make that assumption, there’s not much to be done — they’re hopeless. In reality, people are likely to feel that their intentions are somehow justified (for example, by how they believe you have treated them), and that under the circumstances their behavior is necessary to avoid being taken advantage of. While we may not agree with their views, it is helpful to understand their logic. Because there is a logic, there is also the possibility that you can persuade them that another approach makes sense.

Let’s look at an example.

Colin’s Story

Colin and Matt are fifty–fifty co-owners of a Web design firm. Colin explains:

Matt is usually reasonable enough, but when he really wants something, he uses anger and threats to get it. Recently we were discussing our branding, and suddenly he exploded. “We’re redoing everything! I’m sick of you always playing it safe!” I said that I liked our current direction. He responded with a threat: “I’ll pull the plug and sell my interest in the partnership if we don’t do this my way.” At first when he would do something like that, I would just give in. That helped for a while, but of course Matt would fly into a rage the next time he wanted his way, and I got more and more resentful. So lately, I’ve been trying to give as good as I get: when he yells, I yell louder. But that just escalates the conflict, and now it feels like things are almost out of control.

Colin has tried to manage this problem both by giving in and, more recently, by reacting in kind. Neither has helped.

Giving In. There are times to give in — when you’re persuaded the other person is right; when the other person cares a lot about the outcome and you care little; when any solution is better than no solution and you need an answer immediately. But as a long-term strategy for dealing with difficult behavior, it’s not going to help. Giving in rewards bad behavior, and what gets rewarded gets repeated.

Reacting in Kind: Playing Their Game. There are relationships where reciprocal volatility is the norm — and in some cases, that equilibrium is satisfactory to both sides. Sometimes people say to us, “My husband and I yell and fight all the time, and then we’re over it. Are you saying that’s not okay?” Our response is that if it’s working for both of you, then it’s fine. What matters is that both parties be comfortable with whatever level of engagement occurs and that they make the same meaning out of it.

For many people such relationships are not comfortable. They reason: “For them to treat me this way is a sign of disrespect. They know how upsetting it is to me.” Like Colin, such people can take hours or days to recover from a heated argument or personal attack.

What Might Help

We can see why Colin feels stuck. If giving in rarely helps, and reacting in kind is no better, then what?

First, of course, it can help to imagine how Matt may see the situation and to look for a possible vicious cycle of act-react contributions. For example, perhaps Matt feels that Colin is bullying him by vetoing all change. Matt may even see it as a deliberate — and therefore infuriating — passive-aggressive strategy.

Such insights offer a potentially fruitful new path for discussion. But for our purposes let’s suppose that Matt is sticking with his ultimatum approach, in the belief, conscious or unconscious, that it will work. What else can Colin do?

Name the Dynamic: Make the Trouble Explicit. In chapter 11, we explain the power of this technique. Naming the dynamic is something to try when persistent good faith efforts at listening and problem-solving have failed.

Naming the dynamic requires you to “put on the table as a topic for discussion what you see happening in the conversation itself.” Of particular value is making explicit the other side’s implied rule for how to make decisions. In this case the implied rule that Matt seems to want Colin to adopt is: “When I get upset, I get my way.” Put that way, it’s obvious this would be a lousy way to run a business or maintain a working relationship. The problem becomes even clearer when you apply the test of reciprocity: What if we were both using this strategy? A business is not going to succeed by allocating resources or choosing a marketing campaign based on who can cry or shout the loudest.

When Colin names the dynamic, he should avoid the attribution of intent. He shouldn’t say, “You try to get your way by being angry.” Matt may not recognize or acknowledge that he’s angry, and is very likely to take exception to the idea that he’s simply trying to get his way by throwing a tantrum. That conversation will likely veer off into a pointless argument over Matt’s actual intentions.

Instead, Colin should use the Third Story to describe the dilemma and invite joint problem-solving: “You feel strongly that you want to start over with our branding materials. I like them the way they are. When you and I strongly disagree, how should we decide what to do?” If Matt responds by saying, “Well, if we don’t do it my way, I’m quitting,” Colin can name that dynamic: “So one way we can decide is based on who’s more willing to quit. That doesn’t seem like a sustainable decision-making process or one likely to produce the best decisions. I think a better approach is for us to step back and look again at our goals for these materials, and then to….”

Naming the dynamic is at once neutral (the Third Story) and neutralizing (Colin can’t force Matt to believe what he does not, but neither can Matt force Colin). Colin’s stance is solid and centered: “I’m open to persuasion, but I’m not yet persuaded. And I’m the one who determines when I am. Your anger underscores how strongly you feel, but doesn’t affect the legitimate data, reasoning, and principles that would help persuade me.”

Clarify Consequences. Based on his description, it’s hard to know for sure how upset Colin is. Is Matt’s behavior just an annoyance, or does Colin feel like a punching bag? If the latter, there’s a temptation for Colin to want to give the “enough is enough” speech, where Colin demands that Matt reform his mercurial behavior: “I’ve had it with the yelling and the threats. I’m just not going to put up with it anymore! Enough is enough!”

That sounds good in your head when you’re rehearsing just exactly how to let them have it. The problem comes when you examine carefully whether it’s going to be an effective strategy. While Colin is giving his speech, consider what might be going on in Matt’s internal voice. He may be thinking, “You’re crazy, I don’t get angry” or “More proof that you’re oversensitive” or “Don’t tell me what to do” or “I’m more passionate than you — so what?” And notice, from Matt’s point of view, he was already giving the “enough is enough” speech when he told Colin he was sick of playing it safe. That hasn’t had a very good impact on Colin, and there’s little reason to believe that Colin would achieve more by trying the same thing on Matt.

So we’d recommend something different. Colin can resolve in his mind that enough is enough — nothing wrong with feeling resolute. But rather than trying to control Matt (by actually saying enough is enough), he should focus on what he, Colin, sees, thinks, wants, and will do. Key points might include:

Here’s what I see.

Here’s the impact on me.

You may disagree with my perceptions or feel that your behavior is justified.

It doesn’t matter which one of us is right about that. Our current way of interacting doesn’t work for me.

I am asking you to change this behavior.

If it continues, here’s what I’m going to do.

Colin is not insisting that he is right. He thinks he’s right, but he can’t know he’s right. What he does know is that things aren’t working for him as they are. Colin needs Matt to understand this, not to control Matt, but to give him the information he needs to make informed choices about whether and how to modify his behavior. Of course, to use this approach Colin will have to think carefully about what the real consequences of no change will be and describe them clearly to Matt.

These same strategies are equally helpful with other difficult behaviors. If they are always changing the topic or turning the conversation into an attack on you, work hard to understand their point of view and be open to good faith problem-solving. But if they keep making moves that feel like intentional attempts to derail or bully, name the dynamic (there are more examples in chapter 11), and, if needed, clarify the consequences of no change.

3. What if the other person is particularly hard for me to deal with?

We all have people in our lives whom we find hard to deal with, despite our best efforts. The difficulty may relate to something about you or to something about them. But often, as we say in chapter 4 on joint contribution, the challenge arises from the intersection of each of your different approaches and behaviors. Such challenges may change as we grow, or they may persist over a lifetime. Whatever the cause of the difficulties, these troubled relationships can be enormously upsetting and time consuming. You may feel like you’re the only person in the relationship who is actually trying, and the harder you try, the more resentful you may feel that your efforts are neither reciprocated nor helping.

Consider a low-stakes example: Recently, you asked a colleague how his vacation was, and he fired back that he was “only gone for three days!” His response seemed weirdly defensive until you realized he heard your question as an accusation: “Why were you on vacation for so long?!” As we discuss in chapter 3, we all on occasion attribute intentions to others that are more negative than is warranted by the situation. But some people do it more consistently than most and with greater certainty (“I know it was an accusation!”). In time, you may find yourself avoiding this person, or at least choosing not to ask about their vacations.

We all experience more serious and sustained examples as well. A person with dementia will have cognitive decline, and also may experience troubling shifts in mood. The disease affects people differently; for some, it might lead to wandering from home, or feeling like those closest to them are trying to harm them. The experience for someone with dementia can be profoundly disorienting, even terrifying or humiliating.

The experience for a caregiver or loved ones can be equally challenging. A caregiver may struggle to understand the person they are caring for and feel frustrated, angry, and exhausted when the behavior becomes unpredictable or dangerous. For even the most patient and caring among us, it’s easy to imagine that a person with dementia just isn’t trying hard enough to remember things. Or that their depression would lift if they just had a more positive outlook.

In situations like this good communication skills are necessary — even crucial. But they will rarely be sufficient to “solve” the struggle. As with many relationships, it’s essential to have the support of family, colleagues, and community, as well as relevant health care professionals. And at the end of a long and exhausting day, when you’ve been doing all you can to help, it’s important not to beat yourself up, feeling like you somehow didn’t do enough. In some situations, there simply is no “enough” that would make the challenges disappear.

When we are feeling particularly frustrated with someone, we may be tempted to rely on labels to describe them: They are “impossible” or “irrational.” Or we may assign our own amateur diagnosis to explain their behavior and our frustration, or cite some authority’s actual diagnosis. Such dismissive and global labeling, however, overlooks individual nuance, and can reinforce social narratives that stigmatize mental health issues, and too often result in bias. When possible, refer to a person’s behavior rather than to a label or diagnosis. When we focus on labels rather than behavior, problem-solving also becomes more difficult. Each of us, after all, deserves to be treated as the individual we are.

And remember, even behaviors that are hard to understand or seem irrational may be rooted in a logic that, from the actor’s perspective, makes good sense. If your teenage daughter refuses to leave the house for fear of having a panic attack, aspects of this choice are rational. The choice to stay inside, given the perception of danger outside, is easy to understand. It’s also helpful to remember that the people with whom we are struggling aren’t usually intending to cause us problems, any more than we intend to cause them problems. They are often doing what they can to cope with their own life challenges and ways of seeing the world, just as we are.

With these observations and caveats, let’s consider a few approaches that may help to change difficult dynamics.

Addy’s Story

Addy has a challenging dynamic with her mother’s sister, Addy’s Aunt Robin:

Aunt Robin has a history of telling me how selfish and horrible my mother is, and she tries to get me to agree. She’ll explode in outbursts seemingly out of nowhere, like, “Your mother says I don’t care about the family! Well, your mother’s a goddamn liar!” I calmly explain my mother’s view. Doesn’t help. I’ve also tried sharing impacts: “Robin, when you say things about my mother, I feel upset.” Doesn’t help. I try empathy, acknowledging that she’s been going through a tough time. Doesn’t help. And on occasion, I lose my temper: “How dare you talk about my mother like that!”

That doesn’t help either.

What Might Help

Distinguish Helping from Fixing. Addy knows that Aunt Robin is lonely, and part of her motivation for reaching out to Robin is to help ease those feelings. At least, if asked, this is what Addy would say, and this is what she believes. But when she looks deeper, she finds that her motivation to help Robin is paired with a desire to fix Robin, to get her to conform her behavior to Addy’s view of how people should talk and interact.

But such change is beyond Addy’s control. In her mind, Addy is like Max in Where the Wild Things Are, the seagoing boy-king who sails to an island populated by incorrigible and frightening “wild things.” Max waves his hand and utters the words “be still.” And they are.

Alas, our world doesn’t work that way. While it’s hard to give up this illusion of control, doing so is the first step toward finding an approach that helps.

Think about What Might be Going on for Robin. Something important is at stake for Robin in these conversations, or she wouldn’t keep pursuing them. The connection matters to her. Negative interaction is better than no interaction at all. Robin probably wants to feel loved, that she belongs, and that she has a valued role in the family. In addition, we can assume from her frustration level that Robin feels unheard. She wants to tell someone that she’s upset with her sister and, to her mind, can’t get anyone to listen. Addy needs an approach that acknowledges these core interests and at the same time points the conversation in a different direction.

Consider the Big Reframe. This approach is intended to knock the conversation off its center. That’s usually not a good idea, but if things are stuck or going drastically wrong, sometimes bringing big energy from a different direction can help.

Here’s an example of what Addy might say: “Well, my mother sure can be a pain! Goodness knows, she’s not perfect! Maybe everyone in our family is a pain. But one thing I know is that my mother loves you, and you love her, and I know that means a lot to both of you.”

There are several reasons a statement like this might be useful. First, there’s some humor in it, a sense of lightness that serves as a reminder that it isn’t the end of the world. Second, there’s a strong empathy move at the outset. Addy’s acknowledgement that her mother can be a pain may seem disloyal, but it’s probably true (to the extent that everyone can be sometimes, especially in families). Robin has spent years trying to get someone to move beyond defending her sister and to hear her frustration.

Third, it expresses love. By saying they love each other, Addy reassures Robin that she is loved and gives her an important role in the family, which may help to assuage her fear of being alone.*3

Peter’s Story

Peter is the principal at a public high school, and Lucera is one of his assistant principals. As Peter explains:

Lucera is a gifted teacher and talented administrator. She is extremely hardworking and has great instincts with students. But she’s very difficult to work with. When anyone tries to give her feedback, she rejects it almost aggressively, explaining why it’s wrong, biased, political, whatever. I recently talked to her about several parents who found her to be dismissive of their concerns, and her response in each case was, “Do you trust them more than you trust me?” She then added that she has been cultivating relationships with parents in the community, which is why she found it particularly obnoxious that a few were trying to sabotage her like this.

What Might Help

Recognize Common Gaps in Self-Awareness. Peter’s story suggests that Lucera may be hobbled by a lack of awareness. The signals that communicate anger or defensiveness or disgust to others — tone of voice, facial expressions, body language — lie outside of the communicator’s awareness. The listener is very aware of these; the talker is not. “Yes, I did think they were being ridiculous, but I didn’t say that, so how would they know?” They know because it’s obvious to them; the messages leak out nonverbally — not in what Lucera says but in how she says it.

What this means for Lucera is that she is missing a lot of information about why people are responding to her as they do. From her point of view, she is working hard to be friendly and tolerant, even in the face of all the parental whining. She does not see her own eye rolling, or hear her own tone of voice. When others then say they feel disrespected by her, she is thinking, “But I’m not being disrespectful.” The only explanation she can see is that the parents must be oversensitive or overprotective or that they themselves are difficult people.

Thus, the reason Lucera sees each piece of feedback she gets as unjust is that she actually believes it to be so. It’s the only explanation she can think of.

Two things might help Peter break through Lucera’s immunity to feedback:

Give Lucera the Missing Data. If Lucera agrees to have a conversation videotaped, she may be able to see herself as others do (for many of us, such insights can be jarring or painful). If Lucera can identify what her tone and body language are communicating, it will be easier for her to understand her impact on parents and others in moments like these.

Ask “What if…?” to Consider the Implications. Another thing Peter might say is, “Let’s put aside for a minute the question of whether these complaints are true and instead ask what if they were true. What would it mean? What would be the implications for you?”

As we know, one reason people argue about what happened is that the other person’s view threatens their identity. This approach sidesteps that worry, allowing Peter to help Lucera find a way to feel less threatened by the possibility that her behavior has had unintended consequences.

Mtume’s Story

Mtume is involved in a series of tough conversations with his eighty-six-year-old father about whether his father should continue to drive.

My father has been in several fender benders recently, due in part to his deteriorating vision. When I share my concern that his driving endangers himself and others, he says defiantly (and disturbingly) that I’m overreacting, and if he is hurt or killed, so be it; he’ll drive if he wants to drive. That’s not something he ever would have thought or said even a few years ago. I’ve asked him what his concerns are about not being able to drive, and he says it’s not my business. I’ve also tried stating what I imagine his concerns are (“It must feel like a real loss of freedom.”), but that yields the same line: It’s none of my business. The fear that something bad might happen really weighs on me, but I’m just not sure what to do.

What Might Help

Appeal to Outside Standards or Authorities. This is an example of a conversation where even a skilled mix of real empathy and thoughtful persuasion doesn’t yield the result you want or need. In some circumstances, there may be nothing else to try. In this case, though, Mtume can appeal to an outside authority. If Mtume shares his own observations and concerns about his father’s driving with his father’s doctor — particularly in writing — the doctor can take up the topic with Mtume’s father directly, or require his driving to be reassessed by the state or a local program. Mtume can also report his concerns directly to the state, perhaps anonymously; rules differ across states and countries, but many have a mechanism for flagging drivers that may need reassessment. If Mtume’s father passes the retest, then he may in fact be fit to drive, despite Mtume’s concerns. If he fails, Mtume no longer has to persuade his father. His father may (or may not) continue to insist on driving, but it relieves Mtume of having to be the “bad guy” in the conversation.

Begin Unilateral Problem-Solving. In a context where the other person is doing something that is dangerous or otherwise troubling, it can be useful to start problem-solving as if they agree that there is a problem to be solved, even if they don’t. So here, Mtume can begin to find ways to meet his father’s underlying interests in freedom to get around town and do what needs to be done. He can talk with friends and neighbors to establish alternative means of transport; set up the logistics for driving or delivery services; explore community resources available to seniors who can no longer drive, etc. Concrete solutions can offer peace of mind where vague responses like “we’ll figure it out when the time comes” can’t. And of course, having systems in place will make difficult transitions somewhat easier.

A Final Thought: Consider Setting Boundaries

Sometimes, you can deal with truly abusive or unacceptable behaviors and relationships by closing them down: You can break up with someone, quit a job, go no-contact with a family member, or stop responding to a neighbor’s badgering emails. Other times, by choice or by necessity, you’ll stay in a tough relationship and continue to just do your best.

Of course, your decision doesn’t have to be all or nothing; you can set a boundary anywhere in the space between the two poles of staying or leaving. There are relationships you’ll want to stay in—but only under certain conditions. So, you might ask your colleagues not to email you about work after 7 p.m. Or you might choose to come home for the holidays if your parents agree not to ask you when you’re going to have kids. Or you might let your partner know that you’ll leave the relationship if they don’t go back to rehab.

Conversations about boundaries can be hard, and a boundary that works for you may not work for the other person. But as we’ve seen time and again in this book, sometimes a hard conversation now can save us from a harder conversation later; indeed, sometimes a short-term strain on a relationship can save a relationship. There are no hard-and-fast rules about whether and when to set a boundary,*4 but the questions below can help you sort through your thoughts and feelings:

Are your views and feelings a legitimate part of the relationship?

Is it always you who has to change?

Does the other person attack your character, and not just your behavior?

Is their behavior unrelenting or belittling?

If you do set a boundary, be persistent and patient. If you can, be compassionate and generous in the story you tell yourself about the other person. And be compassionate and generous to yourself; all you can do is your best.

4. What if the other side has more power, or the game is stacked against me?

Confronting a situation where we feel powerless or facing someone who has a lot of power over us can be profoundly uncomfortable. We may feel intimidated, anxious, enraged, nauseous, trapped. And feeling helpless or subject to someone else’s whims can trigger identity issues as well: “Why am I invisible or treated as ‘less than’?” “Why do I have so little control over important things in my life?” or “Help! I feel caught between being seen as a troublemaker or being taken advantage of.”

The reality is that there are many times in life when we won’t get what we want or believe we deserve. The judge awards custody to our spouse; a more extroverted team member gets credit for our work; a physician refuses to give us the diagnosis that would allow us to access state support or a specialist. We use our best skills, we fight hard and with courage, and it’s not enough.

Moreover, for some the fight is tougher than for others, for reasons that are particularly pernicious. Consciously or not, people sometimes make judgments about who is worth listening to based on factors that have nothing to do with the substance or one’s qualifications — factors like skin color, cultural background, height, body type, accent, gender, age, whether one is deaf or hearing, neurotypical or on the spectrum. Your voice may be discounted because you are a recent arrival to the company, community, or country, or because you are a “dinosaur” who is perceived as being out of step with the times. Many of us have had the experience of having our voice and influence — our power — blunted by some facet of who we are or how we are perceived. And some are subject to this kind of discrimination, indignity, and dismissal far more often than others.

Some of us have much more access to opportunities to accumulate financial and social resources, others much less, simply because of the family, geography, or circumstances we were born into. These differences are not just material: if our family and friends know the “rules of the game” for how to move through the dominant culture and make things happen, we are more likely to learn them. If we are welcome and present in the rooms where decisions are made, we are more likely to be heard.

These challenges are real, and skill may only go so far in overcoming them. But it’s also important to note that when we feel we have less power, we are prone to overlooking the power we do have or could have, which may be more than we realize. In our view, the more useful question is not, “Who has more power?” — which can invite overconfidence or hopelessness, depending on the answer — but rather, “In this situation and over time, how can I maximize my ability to influence the other person or the outcome?”

In many situations there is a lot we can do. To see why — and what to do, we first need to understand two different kinds of power in play in any given interaction and how they work.

There Is More to “Power” Than Command and Control

The dictionary defines power as relating to “control, authority, or influence” — in essence, the ability to make things happen. For our purposes it’s useful to distinguish the unilateral ability to make something happen — what we can call self-help or unilateral power — from the ability to influence another’s mind — their thoughts and feelings, attitudes and values, perceptions and preferences, behavior and choices. We’ll call the latter influence or persuasive power.[*5]

Unilateral power is anything you can do without others’ agreement and regardless of what others prefer, think, or feel. Depending on your role and authority, examples might include promoting someone, choosing the starting lineup for the big game, quitting a job, and telling someone you love them.

The three primary sources of unilateral power are resources, role, and a strong walkaway alternative to agreement:

Resources. Financial resources might allow you to build a bigger team, outbid the competition, take more risks, wait out your opponent, or wield political influence. Social resources, like networks of family, friends, school classmates, and work acquaintances, can increase access to jobs, information, and a safety net should things go wrong.

Role. Role refers to your (formal or informal) position within an organization, group, or family (vice president, new member, parent, crossing guard). The higher up the hierarchy, the broader range of decision rights you’ll tend to have for things like grades, shift schedules, hiring, or promotions. Your positionality, as it’s sometimes called, carries consequences for those in your orbit.

A strong walkaway alternative. If we are negotiating and can’t agree, what can we each do instead? In negotiation theory, your “BATNA” (Best Alternative To a Negotiated Agreement) is the best thing you can do “away” from the table — if you don’t come to agreement in this negotiation and decide to walk away. A boss may have unilateral power to decide compensation, but the balance of power in a particular salary conversation will depend in part on the strength of each side’s walkaway alternative: If the employee leaves, how easy would it be for the boss to find a replacement? What are the employee’s job prospects if they were to quit? Do they already have a better offer?

Persuasive power is the ability to persuade someone or to influence how they perceive or think about a situation or decision. I can’t make you love me, but I can bring you flowers, listen when you’re down, and try to make you laugh. I can’t make you work harder, but I might be able to incent you with a bonus or excite you about the possibilities. I can’t force you to vote for my candidate, but I can share information about their policies that you might find appealing.

This book is about both increasing our persuasive power through more skillful communication and stepping into and being willing to use our unilateral power, when appropriate. In most situations we need and draw on both kinds of power to pursue our goals. Doing so skillfully increases our chances of success, but there are limits and no guarantees.

A few key points to facilitate clear thinking and planning:

Power Is Context-Specific. A CEO who sets the direction of her Fortune 500 company may have little ability to quiet her anxious three-year-old at bedtime. The wealthy tourist will likely have to pay more for an item than the cash-strapped exchange student who speaks the language and has learned more of the local cultural norms.

Power Is Fluid, and Can Change in the Course of a Single Interaction. Amazon felt confident soliciting bids from competing cities for new distribution centers; who wouldn’t want thousands of new jobs? Well, it turned out that local commuters and neighbors weren’t excited about adding thousands more cars and trucks to already crowded roadways. They voiced their concerns to local officials, and multiple cities dropped out of the competition, while others offered less attractive terms than Amazon had expected. Then Amazon realized it had preferences for where it wanted to build, and as the remaining cities cottoned on to that, power shifted yet again.

Power Is Not Zero-Sum. Increasing your power does not automatically decrease others’, nor vice versa. In the heat of a conflict, when we’re each threatening to walk away, the stand-off can feel like a power struggle where the first to blink will lose. But if we are able to slow down, listen to each other, and learn what we each care about and why we see it so differently, we each may become more open to persuasion by the other — and have more persuasive power with each other. This happens without either of us giving up our unilateral power to walk away.

Persuasive Power Is Often Required to Achieve Our Goals. As a member of the city council, I have the unilateral power to vote as I see fit, but if I want other council members to vote with me, I’ll have to use persuasion. A union has the unilateral power to go on strike. But the goal of going on strike is not to be on strike, it’s to persuade management to agree to higher wages, better benefits, or improved working conditions. Before, during, and after a strike, union and management will be at the bargaining table, negotiating over the contract, attempting to persuade each other (and perhaps the public).

Our focus here is mostly on the context of one-on-one conversations (like an interaction with an immigration officer deciding your asylum status), but the skills, tools, and mindsets presented generally apply to larger conversations as well, for example, when we are trying to change a policy, culture, or system (like organizing to change the immigration laws). In both cases we are reaching out to engage others who have the ability to help change things — working to build and deploy our own power, and harness theirs, on issues we believe matter most.

Dealing with Powerful Players: Build Your Persuasive Power

So let’s return to the original question: How does this work with a boss or other player who seemingly has most or all of the power? Whether disagreeing with your boss in a meeting, talking with a professor about your grade on an assignment, or petitioning the city council, using the skills in this book surely feels tougher going up the hierarchy than going down.

When we say that the other side has “more” power, we often mean that they have more unilateral power — they hold higher status or rank, are better connected, have greater resources; they are able and willing to flex their authority to make decisions and impose consequences; they are willing to walk away and have strong alternatives if they do. They may be able to fire or transfer you, or give you projects with little appeal, without having to negotiate with you about it.

So what to do?

One thing you can do is build and deploy your persuasive power. It can’t guarantee the outcome you want, but it can increase your odds.

Ways to Enhance Your Persuasive Power

There are many ways you might be able to increase your persuasive power, and it’s useful to consider the potential each of them may hold in your context.

Reach Out to Colleagues to Build Relationships and Test Your Perceptions. Part of what can leave us feeling powerless is feeling alone. “Am I the only person feeling this way? Am I the only person noticing what’s wrong in this situation?” Reach out to others. Share your concerns. Ask for their candid reactions. Invite them to point out what you might be missing. Ask for their advice or their help. If they agree with you that what you are asking for is fair or helpful or reasonable, you’ll feel more confident. And you may gain useful allies who can help raise issues and support efforts for change. One voice may be dismissed. Many are harder to ignore.

Frame the Problem from the Third Story. If you decide to raise the issue with your boss or the powers that be, be sure to use the skills from chapter 8 to start the conversation from the “neutral” Third Story. Frame the challenge as a possible difference in perspective, priorities, predictions, or preferences. Treat the other person as a partner in understanding and managing a problem, rather than someone who is out of touch, or doesn’t care, which will likely trigger identity issues and a desire to defend or attack.

Acknowledge Your Counterpart’s Decision-Making Power. Counterintuitively, explicitly acknowledging that the foreman or department chair is the decision maker can make them more receptive to listening to your input. So you might say, “I know there are lots of factors you have to take into consideration, and at the end of the day, I’m onboard with whatever you decide. I just want to make sure that as you think about it, you are aware that….”

Your counterpart may now be more open to influence, because they don’t need to push back, defend their turf, or clarify that this is their decision to make. You’ve given them a clear signal acknowledging their status, which will help them relax and be receptive.

Say What’s in It for the Boss. What upside might plausibly come from having a conversation that is unlikely without it? For example, you might say, “I want to make this initiative a great success. To do that I need a little more help in making sure I understand the logic well enough to execute effectively.”

Say Why It Matters to You. Now tell your boss what matters to you and why. It’s surprising how often people tell us they’ve “tried everything” to influence their boss, yet when we ask, “Have you told your boss this is important to you?” they respond, “Well, I assume my boss must know.” Being clear that something matters to you, and why, has an impact on the other person. It may or may not persuade them — that will depend on the strength of your argument and competing considerations — but it will have an impact. Perhaps surprisingly research suggests that if the person you’re talking to understands how much you care about a particular topic, they are more open to being influenced by you.

Listen! As we say in chapter 9, listening is not just about taking in information. Listening itself increases your persuasive power in at least two ways.

Listening helps you understand their interests and concerns. The more you understand what the other side cares about, worries about, or hopes for — their interests, constraints, and priorities — the better equipped you will be to find options to which they are more likely to say yes.

You offer reasons why your town should build a dog park, but the town administrator seems unpersuaded. You might be tempted to offer more reasons, argue your case to your neighbors, or start a petition. If instead you first work to understand the official’s concerns, you’ll be much better able to focus your efforts (and arguments) effectively. Is the administrator worried about the cost of building or upkeep, the reaction of neighbors, whether enough people would use it, or liability issues? Perhaps the administrator has heard horror stories from other towns. Once you understand the concerns, you can ask what kind of information or data would be persuasive and then work to address them.

Listening to them makes them more likely to listen to you. Listening well has an impact on the other person — it lets them know that their view is a necessary part of the picture, and that you are open to and interested in better understanding it. Conversational receptiveness on your part sparks receptiveness in your counterpart. They begin to see you as having better judgment, they like you more, and they are more willing to consider what you have to say.[*6] You can’t change their mind, only they can. But when you listen to someone, you help put them in conversation with themselves, which makes a shift in attitude or perceptions more likely.

Of course for this approach to work, you have to actually be open to learning. If your real goal in having a conversation is to show your boss they’re wrong, you’re on dangerous ground, and you’re likely to encounter all the usual problems with delivering a message from a stance of certainty.

Find Creative, Pie-Expanding Options. As one twin demands Frozen and the other Finding Nemo, and your recommendations for turn-taking are going nowhere, your ability to broker agreement with a new idea that both kids prefer can feel like a superpower — hello, Incredibles.

Or perhaps the all-powerful Strategic Initiatives Committee has real doubts about whether your team’s project will work, and turns you down for funding. At first the situation seems hopeless, and your team begins to disband, but then one of your colleagues says, “What if we got our own funding by offering a cut to a strategic partner?” You re-pitch the committee chair, who seems pleasantly surprised by the idea and gives the team the green light to try.

Leverage the Power of Legitimacy. Individuals and institutions care about being — and appearing to be — fair, in line with norms and precedent. So one way to increase your persuasive power is to explain why current practices are hard to justify, and how they need to be changed to be in compliance with the law or the latest thinking, or to be in alignment with the values we espouse. Appealing to current industry norms, case precedents, best practices, or principles of fairness that justify what you’re asking for makes it easier for the other side to agree — and harder for them to continue to disagree. Proposing a fair process for deciding can ensure that more voices are heard, more people have access, and the outcomes achieved are more broadly checked for bias.

Our colleague Ariel Eckblad rightly points out that sometimes current criteria may themselves be unjust and used to protect existing practices of oppression or exclusion. The fact that a minimum wage is state-mandated does not mean that it is a fair or livable wage; failing to provide health insurance may be common in our lowest-wage jobs, but it also perpetuates a cycle of poverty for our most vulnerable.[*7] So tapping the persuasive power of criteria may necessitate challenging “how things are usually done around here” with deeper standards of fairness that tell us why the current practices are unjust — for example, because it violates the principle of equal treatment or denies a fundamental human right.

When competing criteria seem relevant, ask which is more general, widely accepted, evenly applied, aligned with deeply held moral values, or more comfortable to apply whosever foot the shoe is on. It is also useful to ask why there are competing standards. Usually communities and the market coalesce over time around a narrow range of standards. Competing standards often develop when circumstances are changing — something in the context or our understanding of societal impacts is shifting. When this is the case, you can ask whether your situation is more like the traditional context or the emerging one.

Use the Language of Request. When you are pushing for change — whether talking “up” to someone more senior, or trying to change an office culture — the current players, policies, and implicit rules are implicated. And even clever new ideas can be heard as an indictment of how things have been run before, leading to defensive explanations for why what you’re suggesting can’t change.

It’s probably not a good idea to say to the partners, “Look, this mess is your fault” or “Hey, boss, here’s what you’re contributing to our problems.” Rather than the language of blame, or even contribution, try using what we call the language of request. Instead of saying, “Everyone is burned out because you don’t care about the burdens that constant last-minute crises impose on our staff,” say, “I would love to talk about how we can better manage workflow, particularly for complicated projects. Several of us have put together some ideas that we think would enable us to deliver better results, while also protecting staff from burnout. For instance, we’d love to do a midweek status check on key projects. What are your thoughts on that?”

Invest in Building Your Personal Persuasive Power over Time. In addition to deploying your persuasive power as effectively as possible in the moment, there are a number of things you can do to increase your persuasive power over time: foster a reputation for trustworthiness, for being fair, for caring about others, for being a credible expert, or for making wise decisions under pressure. Build a network of caring and mutually supportive relationships. Pay attention to what triggers you emotionally, what helps you cope or feel confident, and what helps you to be open to others even when frustrated or confused, and practice staying balanced.

Each of these strategies will help build more persuasive power to draw on when you need it, because people will view you as reasonable, credible, and someone they trust to get them through a confusing or difficult time. And as a result, you are more likely to be entrusted with additional roles and responsibilities that will increase your unilateral power as well.

Don’t Disempower Yourself: Find Your Unilateral Power

When we’re feeling one-down, when we’re fearful of reprisal or overwhelmed by a differential of rank, status, or experience, it’s often hard to locate our own power. And once we have, we sometimes feel reluctant to use this legitimate unilateral power. When feeling trapped in a miserable job or troubled relationship, when feeling depressed or confused or ambivalent, it’s difficult to see our way clear to imagine a different future. We may be overly optimistic or unreasonably pessimistic about our walkaway prospects.

As the custody battle gets more heated, or as your job burnout becomes more pronounced, as your brother continues to bombard you with nasty, accusatory texts at all hours, you may hesitate to make decisions that are yours to make. We hesitate to say no to more work for fear of not looking like a team player. We let our ex’s bad behavior slide, rather than exercising our right to charge interest on late child support payments. We don’t tell our brother that if he can’t treat us with kindness, we’ll block or simply delete his texts without reading them. We shy away from exercising the unilateral power we do have. We retreat rather than reaching out for help. And the gap between their power and ours only grows larger.

Assessing and using the power embedded in your walkaway alternatives can be tricky. We are sometimes afraid to use our power to walk away or create healthy boundaries, or even to mention our ability to do so, for fear of damaging the relationship. And yet the consequence is that we disempower ourselves, leaving our own unilateral power on the shelf and sacrificing the influence that an honest description of our choices might yield. We also tend to grow more resentful, which damages the relationship anyway. If and when we do quit the job or leave the relationship, the other party may feel ambushed, denied the opportunity to understand how serious it was or to address the underlying issues.

None of this is simple. The risk of retaliation may be real. But if we do nothing, we’re also teaching our counterpart how to successfully keep us in our corner. That won’t change unless we take some steps toward exercising the power that we do have to persuade them that they need to treat us differently.

Although we are proponents of the benefits of good communication, we don’t believe that every problem can be solved with more talk; sometimes it’s appropriate to use your unilateral power. If you’ve already tried a learning conversation — really tried — and gotten nowhere, you have learned something. That learning may lead you to give it one last try, or to exit. You might choose to let your boss know that you have another offer from another employer, and although you’d strongly prefer to remain in your current position, you’re willing to quit if a raise you deem fair is not forthcoming. In a romantic relationship, it’s important for your partner to know that you have needs that are so important to you that you will leave if they are not met. That clarity can sometimes be a gift to both of you to take a hard look at what you each think is — and isn’t — possible.

What If My Boss Simply Refuses to Talk About My Concerns / Our Relationship?

First, on process, it’s crucial when dealing with a difficult boss not to do a hit-and-run conversation. You must schedule time on your boss’s calendar. You need undivided attention and a defined amount of time.

Once in the meeting, how to start? Say what’s on your mind, framing from the Third Story and why it should be of concern to your boss. For example: “I want to talk about how we raise things, as I think it’s affecting morale and productivity.” “I want to talk about the best way to disagree with you in a meeting. What’s your advice?”

You can’t force your boss to talk to you about a particular concern, but you can assertively make the case. This is particularly tough when your boss insists that discussing the matter further is itself a sign of incompetence or troublemaking on your part. Here, it’s crucial to be well prepared. Do the necessary reading or research and try everything you can think of to deal with the issue on your own. This way when you approach your boss, you can say confidently that you’ve exhausted all other angles and that you’re coming to your boss because they are the right person to come to.

What If My Boss Is Abusive?

In his provocatively titled book The No Asshole Rule: Building a Civilized Workplace and Surviving One That Isn’t, Stanford Business School professor Robert Sutton shows readers how to deal with the “bullies, creeps, jerks, tyrants, tormentors, despots, backstabbers, egomaniacs, and all the other [people]” who can make our lives miserable. Citing the emotional, physical, and financial havoc such people wreak, the author’s advice is this: no matter how important such people seem to your business, don’t tolerate them. But of course many businesses do.

There are a variety of reasons why people bully. (By the way, bullies are not always bosses, and studies show that the perpetrator is as likely to be a woman as a man.) They may have been mistreated themselves as a child, believe that such behaviors are simply the best way to get what they want, or assume that their particular business culture requires it. They may suffer from a mental illness or mood disorder. Research shows that people in lower status roles often work hard to manage their stress, moods, and behavior; people in higher-status roles show less inhibition, perhaps because they see others in the organization also getting away with it, or because no one risks speaking up to indicate that it’s a problem.[*8]

Often the person with the difficult behavior is unaware of it. Consider this example: The sales manager learns that your team did not meet its third-quarter targets. He explodes. He verbally attacks you, your teammates, and your customers — anyone who comes to mind as having been responsible for the shortfall. Someone later says to him, “I heard you yelled at the team,” and he is astonished: “I wasn’t yelling; the numbers were bad.” As if only one of these statements could be true at a time. And that’s how he actually experiences it. For him, anger is a self-justifying emotion: it somehow doesn’t count if there’s a cause. Which makes trying to address the issue even more challenging.

Part of the battle with someone who is abusive relates to your own Identity Conversation. Their tactics of anger and shaming can undermine your sense of competence, confidence, and worth. You can doubt your performance, your recollections of what happened, and wonder whether you should trust anyone. You shouldn’t take their feedback at face value — nor should you reject it wholesale. Instead, try to find ways to evaluate your work and their behavior that are independent of the abusive person’s assessment. Easier said than done, we know. But working at it helps.

Sometimes the best survival strategy in the meantime is a workaround. Deal with the person only when required, cultivate as many other relationships as you can, and try to avoid any obvious triggers. In conjunction with that, recognize that most situations are not all or nothing. Professor Sutton eloquently sums up the complexity of dealing with these tough questions. If you feel stuck, you can:

protect your body and mind by reframing the abuse as something that isn’t your fault and won’t magically disappear…. You might also…seek and fight those little battles that you have a good chance of winning.
